Kinship Declarations to Slaves: Father, Mother, Grandfather, Brother, Daughter
Rulings on emancipation by kinship declarations to slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each kinship declaration to a slave (father, mother, grandfather, brother, daughter) and explain the reasoning using the principle of direct or indirect effector in ownership.

Core Idea
A master's kinship declaration to a slave frees only if the relationship directly implies ownership; otherwise, there is consensus or ikhtilaf.

This passage extends the earlier dispute on parent-child declarations to other family relationships. Each term—father, mother, grandfather, brother, daughter—is analyzed based on whether it inherently conveys ownership. For grandfather, the lack of an intermediate father prevents metaphor; for brother, there is a split within the school; for daughter, the gender mismatch leads to consensus against freedom. Understanding these distinctions helps you apply the broader principle of 'effector in ownership' to new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and the slave's attributes
For each declaration, note the specific kinship term and any relevant attributes (age, gender). Example: grandfather declared to a small child; daughter declared to a male slave.
The slave's characteristics determine possibility of literal meaning.
2
Determine the legal effect in ownership (muwjib fil-milk)
Assess whether the declared relationship directly implies ownership. Father/son directly imply ownership because the master having a child necessitates ownership of the mother. Grandfather implies ownership only through the father, which is absent here. Brother and daughter have no direct ownership effect.
This step uses qiyas khafi: subtle analogy based on the presence or absence of a direct link.
3
Assess the possibility of haqiqah or majaz
If the literal meaning is impossible (e.g., calling a male slave 'daughter'), see if it can be interpreted metaphorically. For grandfather, the intermediary needed for metaphor is absent. For brother, it might be considered as implying brotherhood in slavery, which does not necessitate freedom.
The ability to use majaz depends on the existence of a connection in reality or custom.
4
State the ruling and note the source of disagreement
For father/mother, it follows the same ikhtilaf as 'my son'. For grandfather, consensus says no freedom. For brother, zahir al-riwayah says no freedom, but there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that he becomes free. For daughter, consensus says no freedom because the gender mismatches.
The ruling varies; be careful to distinguish between consensus and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال هذا ابى وامى ومثله لا يولد لمثله فهو على هذا الخلاف لمابينا ولوقال لصبي صغير هذا جدى قيل هو على الخلاف وقيل لا يعتق بالاجماع لان هذا الكلام لا موجب له فى الملك الابواسطة وهو الاب وهى غير ثابتة في كلام فتعذر ان يجعل مجازا عن الموجب بخلاف الابوة والبنوة لان لهما موجبافی الملک من غير واسطة ولوقال هذا أخي لا يعتق فى ظاهر الرواية وعن ابي حنيفة انه يعتق ووجه الروايتين مابيناه ولوقال لعبده هذا ابنتى فقد قيل على الخلاف وقد قيل هو بالاجماع لان المشار اليه ليس من جنس المسمى فتعلق الحكم بالمسمى وهو معدوم فلا يعتبر وقد حققناه في النكاح
And if he said (to a slave) 'this is my father' or 'my mother' and such a person cannot be born from such a parent, then it is on this same ikhtilaf based on what we have explained. And if he said to a small child 'this is my grandfather', it is said that it is on the ikhtilaf, and it is said that by consensus he does not become free, because this speech has no effector in ownership except through an intermediary which is the father, and that intermediary is not established in the speech, so it is impossible to make it a metaphor for the effector, unlike fatherhood and sonship because they have an effector in ownership without an intermediary. And if he said 'this is my brother', he does not become free in zahir al-riwayah, and from Abu Hanifa it is narrated that he becomes free, and the basis of the two narrations is what we have explained. And if he said to his slave 'this is my daughter', it is said that it is on the ikhtilaf, and it is said that it is by consensus (no freedom), because the indicated person is not of the same genus as the named, so the ruling attaches to the named which is non-existent, so it is not considered, and we have verified this in nikah.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage extends the previous dispute to other kinship terms. For statements 'this is my father' or 'this is my mother' where it is impossible for such a person to be born from the master, the same ikhtilaf applies as with 'my son' (i.e., Abu Hanifa says freedom, others say no). For 'this is my grandfather' to a small child, there are two opinions: one follows the ikhtilaf, another says by consensus no freedom because grandfatherhood requires an intermediary (the father) which is not established, so metaphor cannot connect to emancipation. For 'this is my brother', in zahir al-riwayah (the relied-upon narrations) there is no freedom, but a narration from Abu Hanifa says he becomes free, with the same basis as before (brotherhood also necessitates freedom through kinship? But the text does not detail the reasoning here, referring to previous explanation). For 'this is my daughter' to a male slave, there is disagreement: some say it follows the ikhtilaf, others say by consensus no freedom because the slave is male, so the literal meaning (daughter) cannot apply, and there is no genus match, so the ruling cannot attach. The text notes it is verified in the chapter of nikah.

Key Terms
بابواسطة (bi-wasitah): Through an intermediary; here, grandfatherhood requires the intermediate link of the father.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative narrations from Abu Hanifa through his major students, constituting the standard school position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is there a consensus that a master's statement 'this is my grandfather' to a small child does not result in emancipation?
A) Because the child is too young to be a grandfather.
B) Because grandfatherhood requires the intermediate link of the father, which is not established in the speech.
C) Because the Sahibayn and Shafi'i all agree it is futile.
D) Because the master's intention cannot be determined.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation through kinship declaration depends on whether the term directly or indirectly implies ownership; grandfatherhood and daughterhood are consensus no, fatherhood/motherhood follow ikhtilaf, brotherhood has intra-Hanafi dispute.

Emancipation by Claiming Impossible Parentage: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation by parentage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute when a master claims an impossible parent-child relationship to a slave and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A claim of parentage that is biologically impossible may not cause emancipation even if intended as a metaphor.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if a master says to a younger slave 'This is my father,' does it free the slave? Imam Abu Hanifa treats the statement as a metaphorical declaration of freedom, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) reject it because the metaphor has no basis in reality. The discussion extends to claims of 'brother,' 'grandfather,' and 'daughter,' each with its own nuance. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying the rules of emancipation by utterance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
Determine the act: a master making a claim of parentage to a slave where the relationship is biologically impossible. Note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether this utterance causes emancipation.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that the utterance causes emancipation because it is a metaphor for freedom, similar to 'this is my son' which definitely frees the slave. The key is that the utterance implies independence, not the truth of the relationship.
This position relies on the principle of majaz (metaphor) in legal language.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn hold that no emancipation occurs because the relationship is impossible, so the metaphor fails. There is no real basis for the statement to be effective. The cause of emancipation (sabab) is absent.
They argue that a metaphor must have a possible literal meaning; here it is impossible.
4
Trace the Extension to Other Relatives
The passage applies the same logic to grandfather (requires intermediary father), brother (possible in reality but still no emancipation in zahir al-riwayah), and daughter (pointed-to male vs. female named). Note where there is consensus or continued dispute.
For daughter, some say consensus that no emancipation occurs because the named does not match the genus.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn (no emancipation) for all these cases, as it is the zahir al-riwayah. Abu Hanifa's view is recorded but not operative for fatwa.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah favors the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مولی نے کہا هذا ابی و امی اور اس جیسا ان سے پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا
و لو قال هذا ابى وامى ومثله لا يولد لمثله فهو على هذا الخلاف لمابينا ولوقال لصبي صغير هذا جدى قيل هو على الخلاف وقيل لا يعتق بالاجماع لان هذا الكلام لا موجب له فى الملك الابواسطة وهو الاب وهى غير ثابتة في كلام فتعذر ان يجعل مجازا عن الموجب بخلاف الابوة والبنوة لان لهما موجبافی الملک من غير واسطة ولوقال هذا أخي لا يعتق فى ظاهر الرواية وعن ابي حنيفة انه يعتق ووجه الروايتين مابيناه ولوقال لعبده هذا ابنتى فقد قيل على الخلاف وقد قيل هو بالاجماع لان المشار اليه ليس من جنس المسمى فتعلق الحكم بالمسمى وهو معدوم فلا يعتبر وقد حققناه في النكاح
ترجمہ اور اگر ایسے غلام کو یا لونڈی کو ) جس سے یہ خود پیدا نہیں وہ سکتا ہے کہا کہ یہ میرا باپ ہے۔ یا ( یہ ) میری ماں ہے۔ تو بھی یہی اختلاف ہے۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے اور اگر مالک نے کسی چھوٹے بچے کو کہا کہ یہ میرا دادا ہے تو کہا گیا کہ یہ بھی اسی اختلاف پر ہے اور کہا گیا کہ بالا تفاق آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ملک میں اس کلام سے کوئی بات لازم نہیں آتی مگر باپ کے واسطے سے اور واسطہ اس کلام میں ثابت نہیں ہے۔ تو یہ بات محال ہے کہ اس کلام کو مجازاً آزادی کا موجب قرار دیا جائے۔ برخلاف باپ ہونے اور بیٹا ہونے کے اسلئے ان دونوں باتوں میں مملوک میں بغیر کسی واسطہ کے آزادی کا موجب ہے اور اگر کہا کہ یہ میرا بھائی ہے تو ظاہر الروایۃ میں آزاد نہ ہوگا اور ابوحنیفہ سے ایک روایت ہے کہ آزاد ہو جائے گا اور دونوں روایتوں کی وجہ ہم بیان کر چکے اور اگر اپنے غلام کو کہا کہ یہ میری بیٹی ہے تو کہا گیا کہ (یہ بھی ) مختلف فیہ ہے اور کہا گیا کہ مجمع علیہ ہے۔ کیونکہ مشار الیہ سمی کی جنس سے نہیں ہے۔ پس حکم کا تعلق مسمیٰ سے ہو گا۔ حالانکہ وہ معدوم ہے۔ تو کلام معتبر نہیں ہو گا اور ہم اس کو کتاب النکاح میں ثابت کر چکے ہیں۔
The master said 'this is my father and mother' and someone like him cannot be born from them. 'And if he said, 'This is my father and mother,' and someone like him cannot be born from him, then it is on this disagreement because of what we have explained. And if he said to a young boy, 'This is my grandfather,' it is said it is on the disagreement, and it is said he is not emancipated by consensus because this speech has no effect in property except through an intermediary, which is the father, and the intermediary is not established in the speech, so it is impossible to make it a metaphor for the cause, unlike paternity and sonship because they have a cause in property without an intermediary. And if he said, 'This is my brother,' he is not emancipated in the apparent narration, and from Abu Hanifa that he is emancipated, and the reason for the two narrations we have explained. And if he said to his slave, 'This is my daughter,' it is said it is on the disagreement, and it is said it is by consensus because the pointed-to is not of the same genus as the named, so the ruling is attached to the named, and it is non-existent, so it is not considered, and we have verified it in the Book of Marriage. Translation: And if he said to such a slave or slave-girl from whom he himself cannot be born, 'This is my father' or 'This is my mother,' then this same disagreement applies, due to the evidence we have stated. And if the master said to a young child, 'This is my grandfather,' it was said that this is also on the disagreement, and it was said that by consensus he will not be emancipated, because in property this speech causes nothing except through the intermediary of the father, and the intermediary is not established in the speech. So it is impossible to make this speech metaphorically the cause of freedom, contrary to fatherhood and sonship, because both of these cause freedom in property without any intermediary. And if he said, 'This is my brother,' then in the apparent narration he will not be emancipated, and from Abu Hanifa there is a narration that he will be emancipated, and the reason for both narrations we have explained. And if he said to his slave, 'This is my daughter,' it was said that (this also) is disputed, and it was said that it is unanimous, because the pointed-to is not of the same genus as the named. So the ruling will be attached to the named, while it is non-existent, so the speech will not be considered. And we have verified this in the Book of Marriage.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding emancipation when a master claims a relationship to a slave that is biologically impossible. The case: a master says to a slave (or slave-girl) 'This is my father' or 'This is my mother' when the slave is younger and cannot have fathered or mothered the master. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this utterance results in emancipation, because it is a declaration that the slave is free (since a father cannot be owned). This is based on the principle that claiming a relationship that implies freedom is effective even if metaphorical, as seen in the case of saying 'This is my son.' However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that emancipation does not occur, because the statement is pure metaphor without a basis in reality; the slave cannot actually be the father, so the metaphor fails. The passage then extends this logic to other relational claims: grandfather, brother, and daughter. For grandfather, some say the same disagreement applies, but others say there is consensus that emancipation does not occur because the grandfather relationship requires the intermediary of the father, which is absent. For brother, in the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) position, there is no emancipation, but a narration from Abu Hanifa holds that he is freed—the disagreement mirrors the earlier reasoning. For daughter, similarly there are two views; some say it is disputed, others say it is unanimous that the slave is not freed because the pointed-to (the male slave) is not of the same genus as the named (female daughter), so the utterance is void.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave, the legal consequence of certain utterances.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word; here, an utterance used to imply freedom rather than literal meaning.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known transmission from the imams, usually referring to the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, if a master says to his young slave 'You are my father,' what is the ruling?
A) The slave is emancipated because the utterance implies freedom.
B) The slave is not emancipated because the statement is biologically impossible and cannot be taken as a metaphor.
C) The slave is emancipated only if the master also says 'I free you.'
D) The slave is considered a free person from the moment of utterance, but must pay his value.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a declaration of parentage that is biologically impossible generally does not effect emancipation, based on the Sahibayn's position in the apparent narrations.

Saying 'This is my Grandfather' to a Child Slave
Explains the ruling on emancipation when calling a young boy 'my grandfather'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the grandfather claim fails to emancipate due to the missing intermediary, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the matter.

Core Idea
Grandfatherhood requires a father intermediary; without it, the metaphor cannot trigger emancipation.

This case extends the previous dispute from father/mother claims to the grandfather. Some Hanafi scholars argued that the same disagreement applies, while others asserted a consensus that no emancipation occurs. The key distinction lies in the necessity of an intermediary for the grandfather relationship, which is absent in the utterance. Understanding this reasoning sharpens your ability to apply the principle of intermediary (wasitah) in ownership contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and legal issue
What did the master say? To whom? What is the legal consequence being debated?
The act is an utterance meant to imply a relationship, and the issue is whether it frees the slave.
2
State the two positions
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some say the same father/mother disagreement applies; others claim consensus that no emancipation occurs.
The first position analogizes to the previous case; the second emphasizes the missing intermediary.
3
Analyse the reasoning for the consensus view
Why does the grandfather relationship require an intermediary? How does the absence of the father in the utterance make the metaphor impossible?
The text states that the speech has no effect in ownership without the father as intermediary.
4
Compare with the father/son case
Why does paternity/sonship have a direct effect? Because those relationships directly create ownership implications without an intermediary.
This contrast is the basis for distinguishing the grandfather case.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The stronger view (mufta bihi) is that the slave is not emancipated, as the intermediary condition is missing.
The consensus claim strengthens this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال لصبي صغير هذا جدى قيل هو على الخلاف وقيل لا يعتق بالاجماع لان هذا الكلام لا موجب له فى الملك الابواسطة وهو الاب وهى غير ثابتة في كلام فتعذر ان يجعل مجازا عن الموجب بخلاف الابوة والبنوة لان لهما موجبافی الملک من غير واسطة
And if he said to a young boy, 'This is my grandfather,' it is said it is on the disagreement, and it is said he is not emancipated by consensus because this speech has no effect in property except through an intermediary, which is the father, and the intermediary is not established in the speech, so it is impossible to make it a metaphor for the cause, unlike paternity and sonship because they have a cause in property without an intermediary.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This short passage addresses a specific scenario: a master says to a young male slave, 'This is my grandfather.' Since the boy is biologically incapable of being a grandfather to the master (being much younger), the utterance is impossible literally. The dispute is whether it can be taken as a metaphor for freedom, similar to the father/mother case. Some Hanafi scholars say the same disagreement applies: Abu Hanifa would free the slave, the Sahibayn would not. However, others hold that there is consensus (ijma) among the Hanafis that no emancipation occurs. The reasoning is that the relationship of grandfatherhood requires an intermediary—the father. The utterance 'This is my grandfather' implicitly requires a father in between, but that father is not mentioned and does not exist in the ownership context. Since the cause of freedom (the intermediary) is absent, the metaphor cannot be sustained. This is unlike 'father' or 'son' where the relationship directly creates ownership implications without an intermediary. Therefore, the stronger view is that the slave is not freed.

Key Terms
واسطة (waasitah): An intermediary; here, the father is the necessary intermediary for the grandfather relationship.
بالإجماع (bil-ijma'): By consensus; here, some scholars claim consensus among Hanafis that no emancipation occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do some Hanafi scholars say that calling a young boy 'my grandfather' does not cause emancipation by consensus?
A) Because grandfatherhood requires paternity to be established, which is missing, so the metaphor fails.
B) Because the boy is too young to be a grandfather, so the statement is simply false.
C) Because the master did not specifically say 'you are free.'
D) Because the grandfather relationship is not recognized in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
The grandfather relationship requires a father intermediary; without it, the metaphor for emancipation fails.

Saying 'This is my Brother' to a Slave
States the ruling and a narrated difference regarding emancipation by claiming brotherhood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) ruling when a master says 'this is my brother' to a slave, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Claiming brotherhood does not automatically free a slave in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

The act of a master calling his slave 'my brother' raises the question: does this utterance imply emancipation? The Hanafi school has two transmissions: the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah) holds it does not free the slave, while a narration from Abu Hanifa treats it as a metaphor for freedom. Understanding why the school prefers one over the other is essential for applying the hierarchy of Hanafi legal authority.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) ruling: no emancipation.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opponent is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa himself: emancipation occurs.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The reasoning: brotherhood implies equality and freedom, similar to claiming paternity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Sahibayn and the school argue that brotherhood does not inherently negate slavery, as a free man can be brother to a slave.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the master says this, is the slave free or not? The mufta bihi is no emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال هذا أخي لا يعتق فى ظاهر الرواية وعن ابي حنيفة انه يعتق ووجه الروايتين مابيناه
And if he said, 'This is my brother,' he is not emancipated in the apparent narration, and from Abu Hanifa that he is emancipated, and the reason for the two narrations we have explained.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where a master says to his slave, 'This is my brother.' The apparent (zahir al-riwayah) position is that emancipation does not occur. However, there is a transmission from Abu Hanifa stating that the slave is freed. The author notes that the reasoning for both views has been explained earlier. The basis for the difference lies in whether the utterance can be interpreted as a metaphor for freedom. Brotherhood, like paternity, is a relationship that implies equality and freedom; however, it is not as strong as paternity because a brother is not necessarily free (a slave can have a free brother). The Sahibayn hold that the mere claim of brotherhood, without more, does not necessitate freedom because the brother relationship does not inherently contradict ownership. Abu Hanifa, in this narration, considers it a strong metaphor that implies freedom, similar to claiming 'this is my son.'

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The clear, well-known transmitted ruling from the Hanafi imams, specifically from the six books of Muhammad al-Shaybani.
رواية (riwayah): A transmission or narration from an imam; here, a narration from Abu Hanifa that differs from the apparent one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) in the Hanafi school, if a master says 'This is my brother' to his slave, what is the ruling?
A) The slave is emancipated because brotherhood implies freedom.
B) The slave is not emancipated.
C) The slave is emancipated only if the master also says 'and he is free.'
D) The slave is considered a free person but must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mere claim of brotherhood does not free a slave; only clear words of emancipation suffice.

Genus Mismatch and Emancipation: Calling a Male Slave 'My Daughter'
Intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of a gender-mismatched statement on emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master calls a male slave 'my daughter' and explain the rationale based on the genus (jins) principle.

Core Idea
A male slave is not of the same genus as a daughter, so the utterance is void and no emancipation occurs.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a peculiar case: a master points to his male slave and says, 'This is my daughter.' The slave is male, but 'daughter' is female. Does such a statement effect emancipation? The text reports two intra-Hanafi views and provides the reasoning for the preferred consensus view. Understanding this ruling reveals a key Hanafi principle about the role of genus (jins) in legal utterances, which has applications beyond this case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and the ikhtilaf
The act is a master saying 'This is my daughter' to his male slave. The ruling in question is whether this statement causes the slave to be emancipated. Two views are reported: one that it falls under the same disagreement as the father/mother case, and another that there is consensus that no emancipation occurs.
The father/mother case refers to a master calling a slave 'my father' or 'my mother', which is disputed among the early Hanafis.
2
Understand the consensus view's reasoning (talil)
The consensus view argues that the pointed-to object (the male slave) is not of the same genus (jins) as the named entity (daughter). In Hanafi legal theory, when a designation is made, the ruling attaches to the named entity (musamma). Since the named entity (daughter) is completely absent (the slave is male), the utterance is considered void and has no legal effect.
The term 'jins' here refers to gender categories; male and female are distinct genera.
3
See how this principle is applied in nikah
The author notes that this principle has been verified in the Book of Marriage (nikah). For example, if a man says to a woman 'You are my wife' but the woman is not actually his wife, the statement is void because the designated entity (wife) is not present. Similarly, here the designated 'daughter' is not present, so the statement is void.
This cross-reference shows the consistency of Hanafi legal reasoning across different chapters.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author favors the consensus view, and it is the relied-upon position for fatwa. Therefore, the slave is not emancipated by such a statement.
The other view, which analogizes to the father/mother dispute, is considered less strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال لعبده هذا ابنتى فقد قيل على الخلاف وقد قيل هو بالاجماع لان المشار اليه ليس من جنس المسمى فتعلق الحكم بالمسمى وهو معدوم فلا يعتبر وقد حققناه في النكاح
And if he said to his slave, 'This is my daughter,' it is said it is on the disagreement, and it is said it is by consensus because the pointed-to is not of the same genus as the named, so the ruling is attached to the named, and it is non-existent, so it is not considered, and we have verified it in the Book of Marriage.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a master says to his male slave, 'This is my daughter.' Since the slave is male and 'daughter' is female, there is a clear gender mismatch. Two views are reported: one that it follows the same disagreement as in the father/mother case (i.e., Abu Hanifa may free him, the Sahibayn not), and another view that there is consensus that the slave is not freed. The reasoning for the consensus view is that the object pointed to (the male slave) is not of the same genus (jins) as the named entity (daughter). In Hanafi law, when a designation is made, if the subject and predicate mismatch in genus, the ruling is attached to the named entity (the daughter), which is completely absent here. Therefore, the utterance is void and cannot effect emancipation. The author notes that this principle has been verified in the Book of Marriage (nikah), likely referring to a rule about verbal contracts.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus or category; here, male and female are considered different genera.
مسمى (musamma): The named entity; here, the daughter that is named but not present.

Key Takeaway
Calling a male slave 'my daughter' does not effect emancipation because the genus mismatch makes the utterance void; the ruling attaches to the non-existent named entity.

Rulings on Master's Statements of Lineage and Emancipation
Explains intra-Hanafi disputes on emancipation through lineage claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions for each lineage statement (father, mother, brother, daughter) and explain the reasoning behind the disputes between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A master's claim of paternity frees a slave only if directly possible; lineage through an intermediary is not enough.

When a master declares a lineage relationship to his slave, does that set the slave free? The Hanafi school debates this across different relationships and conditions. This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah dissects the disputes between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, and the reasoning behind each scenario, including the use of qiyas for the daughter claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf for each relationship
List the four relationships mentioned: father/mother (younger or equal-aged slave), father (minor slave), brother, daughter. Note that each has its own dispute or consensus.
The father/mother case has a clear ikhtilaf; minor slave case has two views; brother case has a narration difference; daughter case has a consensus view likely.
2
Determine the positions of Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
For father/mother: Abu Hanifa says free, Sahibayn say not free. For brother: Zahir al-Riwayah says not free, but a narration from Abu Hanifa says free. For daughter: some say disputed, others say by agreement not free.
The Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the father/mother dispute
The source mentions that proofs have already passed. The key concept is whether the statement directly implies sonship (which is a cause of freedom) or requires an intermediary (like when the claimed father is biologically impossible).
Abu Hanifa considers the claim itself as sufficient; Sahibayn require biological possibility.
4
Analyze the intermediary argument for the minor slave case
For a minor slave, some scholars say there is no dispute because the statement 'this is my father' can only be effective through an intermediary (the father), which is not mentioned. Therefore, the slave is not freed by consensus.
This is opposed to the direct claim 'this is my son' which does not need an intermediary.
5
Analyze the qiyas for the daughter claim
The source draws a parallel to a previous case: if a contract specifies a named thing (e.g., wine) but points to a different type, the ruling attaches to the named thing. Since the pointed-to slave is male and the named 'daughter' is female, and male/female are different types in law, the named thing (daughter) is non-existent, so the statement is void.
This qiyas leads to the conclusion that the slave is not freed.

Source Text

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مولیٰ نے ایسے غلام کو یا باندی کو جس سے یہ خود پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے کہا کہ یہ میرا باپ ہے یا یہ میری ماں ہے۔ حالانکہ یہ دونوں عمر میں اس سے چھوٹے ہیں یا برابر یا ایک دو سال بڑے ہیں تو بھی یہی اختلاف ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کلام سے آزادی واقع ہو جائے گی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ فریقین کے دلائل سابق میں گذر چکے ہیں ۔ اور اگر مولی نے اپنے غلاموں میں اس کلام سے کوئی بات لازم نہیں آتی مگر باپ کے واسطے سے۔ یعنی جب باپ کے واسطے سے لیا جائے تب دادا کا حق ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ مولی کے کلام میں اس واسطہ کا کوئی ذکر نہیں ہے۔ تو یہ بات محال ہو گئی کہ اس کلام کو مجاز آزادی کا موجب ٹھہرایا جائے۔ برخلاف اس قول کے کہ یہ میرا باپ ہے یا یہ میرا بیٹا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ دونوں باتیں مملوک میں بغیر کسی واسطہ کے آزادی واجب کرتی ہیں اور اگر مالک نے غلام کو کہا کہ یہ میرا بھائی ہے تو ظاہر الروایۃ کے حکم کے مطابق آزاد نہ ہوگا اور ابو حنیفہ سے یہ بھی روایت ہے کہ آزاد ہو جائے گا اور دونوں روایتوں کی وجہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ چنانچہ آزادی کی روایت کی وجہ لان البنوة فى المملوک سبب الحرية سے اور عدم محقق کی وجہ لأن هذا الكلام لا موجب له في الملک الا بواسطة سے بیان کی گئی ہے۔ ملاحظہ کر لیا جائے اور اگر اپنے غلام سے کہا کہ یہ میری بیٹی ہے تو بعض کا خیال ہے کہ یہ جو مشار الیہ ہے اور لڑکی جو سمی ہے دونوں کی جنس میں اختلاف ہے اور اختلاف جنس کی صورت میں حکم مسمی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ اس مسئلہ میں مسمی یعنی لڑکی معدوم ہے۔ لہذا یہ کلام غیر معتبر ہوگا نہ حقیقتا معتبر ہوگا اور نہ مجازا۔ پس جب یہ کلام بالکل ہی غیر معتبر ہوا تو اس سے غلام بھی آزاد نہ ہو گا۔
Explanation of the issue: If the master says to a slave or slave-girl from whom he himself cannot be born, 'This is my father' or 'This is my mother,' even if both are younger than him in age, or equal, or one or two years older, then there is this same dispute: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, emancipation occurs by this speech, and according to the Sahibayn, emancipation does not occur. The proofs of both parties have already passed earlier. And if the master says to any minor child among his slaves, 'This is my father [؟],' then in this case some mashayikh said that this dispute also applies, and some said there is no dispute, rather by agreement he will not be free. The proof is that no consequence follows from this speech regarding the slave except through the intermediary of the father. That is, when it is taken through the father, then the grandfather's right is established. However, in the master's speech, there is no mention of this intermediary. So it becomes impossible that this speech be considered a cause of emancipation. Unlike the statement 'This is my father' or 'This is my son,' because both these statements necessitate emancipation without any intermediary. And if the owner says to the slave, 'This is my brother,' then according to the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah, he will not be free, and from Abu Hanifa it is also narrated that he will be free. We have already explained the reason for both narrations. Accordingly, the reason for the narration of freedom is from 'because sonship in the slave is a cause of freedom,' and the reason for non-occurrence is from 'because this speech has no effect in ownership except through an intermediary.' May it be noted. And if he says to his slave, 'This is my daughter,' then some think that this case is also disputed between Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn, and some said that in this case by agreement the slave will not be free. The proof of not being free is that in the chapter of mabr, under the issue 'For he married a woman for this vessel of vinegar, and it turned out to be wine,' it has passed that if the pointed-to thing is not of the same type as the named thing, then the ruling is attached to the named thing. And since in humans, male and female are two different types due to difference in purposes, therefore in this issue, the slave who is the pointed-to thing and the girl who is the named thing, there is a difference in their type. And in the case of difference in type, the ruling is attached to the named thing. However, in this issue, the named thing, i.e., the girl, is non-existent. Hence, this speech will be invalid, neither valid as a reality nor as a metaphor. So when this speech is completely invalid, the slave will not be freed by it.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a master's claim of lineage towards his slave causes emancipation. The author presents multiple scenarios: (1) claiming 'this is my father/mother' when the slave is younger or of similar age – Imam Abu Hanifa holds emancipation, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not. (2) For a minor slave, some scholars extend the dispute; others say unanimous non-emancipation because the effect requires an intermediary (the father) not mentioned. (3) Claiming 'this is my brother' – per Zahir al-Riwayah, no emancipation; a narration from Abu Hanifa says yes. (4) Claiming 'this is my daughter' – two views: some say disputed, others say unanimous non-emancipation based on a qiyas from a previous issue about type mismatch (the pointed-to object and named object must match in genus; since male and female are different genera, the named 'daughter' is non-existent, so the statement is null). The reasoning centers on whether the statement directly implies paternity/sonship (which is a cause of freedom) or requires an intermediary.

Key Terms
بنوة (bunuwwa): Sonship; the legal relationship of being a son, which in the context of a slave can be a cause of emancipation if acknowledged by the master.
صاحبین (sahibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
مملوک (mamluk): A slave owned by another person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his minor slave 'You are my father,' what is the ruling according to some mashayikh?
A) The slave is freed by consensus.
B) The slave is not freed by consensus.
C) There is a dispute as in the case of an adult slave.
D) The slave is freed only if the master intended it.

Key Takeaway
A master's lineage statement frees the slave only if it directly establishes paternity or sonship without needing an intermediary; otherwise, the statement is ineffective.

Rulings on Phrases Resembling Emancipation
Rulings for each phrase a master may use regarding his slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the four phrases (anta mithl al-hurr, ma anta illa al-hurr, ra'suka ra'su hurr, ra'suka ra'sun hurr) and explain the linguistic reasoning that determines whether emancipation occurs.

Core Idea
Linguistic precision determines legal effect: simile creates doubt and blocks emancipation, while emphatic affirmation removes doubt and effects freedom.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition examines four specific utterances a master might use regarding his slave. The rulings hinge on careful linguistic analysis: when is a statement a mere simile, and when is it an emphatic declaration of freedom? You will learn how the Hanafi school parses these phrases to determine whether emancipation occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its key linguistic feature
For each of the four utterances, note the key word or structure: mithl (simile), istithna min al-nafi (exception after negation), idafa (construct phrase) with omitted simile particle, or tawsif (descriptive compound).
2
Determine whether the phrase creates doubt or certainty
Analyze the linguistic feature: does it indicate full identity or mere similarity? 'Mithl' implies sharing in some attributes, not total identity, thus creating doubt. 'Ma anta illa al-hurr' is an emphatic affirmative like the Shahada, leaving no doubt. The idafa phrase is a hidden simile; the tawsif is a direct attribution.
Doubt prevents emancipation; certainty effects it.
3
Apply the principle: doubt prevents freedom
For phrases that create doubt (mithl, idafa simile), the slave is not freed. For phrases that affirm unequivocally (istithna, tawsif), the slave is freed.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding intention
For 'anta mithl al-hurr', the author of al-Inaya holds that intention does not affect the ruling, while al-Mabsut states that if the master intends emancipation, doubt is removed and the slave is freed. The preferred view is that intention does not change the outcome.
This ikhtilaf shows nuance in how later scholars treated the role of intention.
5
Derive the ruling for each phrase
Synthesize: (1) 'anta mithl al-hurr' → not freed; (2) 'ma anta illa al-hurr' → freed; (3) 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (idafa) → not freed (hidden simile); (4) 'ra'suka ra'sun hurr' (tawsif) → freed (direct attribution, head representing whole body).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد
۲۹۵
کتاب
مولیٰ نے اپنے غلام کو کہا انت مثل الحر آزاد نہیں ہوگا
واذا قال لعبده انت مثل الحر لم يعتق لان المثل يستعمل للمشاركة في بعض المعنى عرفا فوقع الشک فی الحرية ولوقال ما انت الاحر عتق لان الاستثناء من النفى اثبات على وجه التاكيد كما في كلمة الشهادة و لوقال راسک راسَ حرلا يعتق لانه تشبيه بحذف حرفه ولوقال راسک راس حرعتق لانه اثبات الحرية فيه اذ الرأس يعبر به عن جميع البدن
ترجمہ اور اگر اپنے غلام سے کہا تو آزاد کے مثل ہے تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ لفظ مثل عرفاً بعض معانی میں مشارکت کے واسطے آتا ہے۔ تو (اس کے ) آزاد ہونے میں شک ہو گیا اور اگر کہا کہ تو نہیں ہے مگر آزاد تو وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ نفی سے استثناء کرنا تاکید کے طور پر اثبات ہو جاتا ہے۔ جیسے کلمہ شہادت میں ہے اور اگر کہا کہ تیرا سر آزاد کا سر ہے تو وہ آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ کیونک حرف تشبیہ حذف کرنے کے ساتھ یہ تشبیہ ہے اور اگر کہا کہ تیرا سر آزادسر ہے تو وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ کہنا اپنے غلام میں آزادی ثابت کرنا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ سر کے ساتھ تمام بدن تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ! اگر مولی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا انت مثل الحر تو غلام آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ اشل عرف عام میں بعض اوصاف میں مشترک ہونے کے واسطے آتا ہے۔ پس معلوم نہیں کہ غلام کو آزاد کے ساتھ کسی وصف میں تشبیہ دی گئی ہے۔ اس وجہ سے آزاد ہونے میں شک ہو گیا اور شک کی وجہ سے آزادی واقع نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اسلئے اس کلام سے غلام آزاد نہیں ہو گا۔ صاحب عنایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مصنف کی عبارت کے مطلق ہونے سے ظاہر ہوتا ہے کہ اس صورت میں غلام آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ خواہ آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی ہو یا نہ کی ہو اور مبسوط میں مذکور ہے کہ اگر نیت نہیں کی ہے تب تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ لیکن اگر آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی ہے تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آزادی کا واقع نہ ہو نا شک کی وجہ سے تھا۔ مگر جب آزاد کرنے کی نیت کر لی تو شک زائل ہو گیا۔ اور اگر مولیٰ نے اپنے غلام سے کہا ما انت الاحر تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ نفی سے استثناء کرنا تاکید کے طور پر اثبات ہے جیسے کلمہ شہادت لا الہ الا اللہ میں ہے ۔ یعنی لا الہ الا اللہ کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ اللہ ہی الوہیت والا ہے۔ اسی طرح ما انت الاحر کے معنی ہیں کہ تو ضرور آزاد ہے اور اگر مولیٰ نے کہا راسک راس حر اضافت کے ساتھ تو وہ آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ حرف تشبیہ حذف کرنے کے ساتھ یہ تشبیہ ہے۔ اصل عبارت تھی راسک کرأس حر اور پہلے مسئلہ انت مثل الـحـر میں گذر چکا کہ غلام آزاد نہیں ہو گا۔ حالانکہ تشبیہ موجود ہے۔ پس ایسے ہی اس مسئلہ میں بھی آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔
اور اگر مولی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا راسک راس حر ترکیب توصیفی کے ساتھ تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں راُس کے اندر آزادی ثابت کی گئی ہے اور راس ان اعضاء انسانی میں سے ہے جس کے ساتھ پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے اور سابق میں یہ ضابطہ بیان ہو چکا کہ جس عضو سے پورا بدن تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ اس کی طرف اگر آزدی منسوب کی جائے تو پورا غلام آزاد ہو جاتا ہے
جیسا کہ طلاق میں ہے۔
Ashraf al-Hidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Vol 295. Book. The master said to his slave: 'You are like a free person' — he will not be freed. And if he said to his slave: 'You are like a free person' he is not freed because the word 'like' (mithl) is customarily used for sharing in some meaning, so doubt occurred in freedom. And if he said: 'You are nothing but free' he is freed because exception from negation is affirmation in the mode of emphasis, as in the word of testimony (kalimat al-shahada). And if he said: 'Your head is the head of a free person' (with idafa) he is not freed because it is a simile with omission of the particle (harf tashbih). And if he said: 'Your head is a free head' (descriptive compound) he is freed because it is establishing freedom in it, since the head is used to refer to the entire body. Translation: And if he said to his slave: 'You are like a free person' then he will not be freed. Because the word 'mithl' customarily comes for partnership in some meanings, so doubt occurred in his being free. And if he said: 'You are nothing but free' then he will be freed, because exception from negation is affirmation in the mode of emphasis, as in the word of testimony. And if he said: 'Your head is the head of a free person' (with idafa) then he will not be freed, because it is a simile with omission of the particle of simile. And if he said: 'Your head is a free head' then he will be freed, because this saying establishes freedom in his slave, since the head is used to express the entire body. Clarification of the case: If the master said to his slave: 'You are like a free person', the slave will not be freed. The proof is that the word 'mithl' in common custom is used for sharing in some attributes. So it is not known in which attribute the slave was likened to a free person. Due to this, doubt occurred in freedom, and due to doubt, freedom does not occur. Therefore, the slave will not be freed by this speech. The author of al-'Inaya said that from the absoluteness of the author's expression it appears that in this case the slave will not be freed, whether he intended to free or not. And in al-Mabsut it is mentioned that if he did not intend, then he will not be freed, but if he intended to free, then the slave will be freed, because the non-occurrence of freedom was due to doubt, and when he intended to free, the doubt was removed. And if the master said to his slave: 'You are nothing but free' then the slave will be freed. Because exception from negation is affirmation in the mode of emphasis, as in the testimony 'La ilaha illa Allah' — meaning only Allah is worthy of worship. Similarly, 'ma anta illa al-hurr' means 'You are definitely free'. And if the master said: 'Your head is the head of a free person' (with idafa) then he will not be freed, because it is a simile with omission of the particle. The original phrase was 'ra'suka ka-ra'si hurr'. And in the previous case of 'anta mithl al-hurr' it has passed that the slave will not be freed despite the simile existing. So likewise in this case, he will not be freed. And if the master said to his slave: 'Your head is a free head' (descriptive compound) then the slave will be freed. Because in this form, freedom is established within the head, and the head is among the human limbs by which the entire body is expressed. And earlier it has been stated as a principle that if freedom is attributed to a limb that stands for the whole body, the entire slave becomes free, as in divorce.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on specific phrases uttered by a master concerning his slave, determining whether emancipation (itq) occurs. It is a parsing exercise that distinguishes between literal similes, emphatic negations, and descriptive constructs. The author presents four cases: (1) 'Anta mithl al-hurr' — the slave is not freed because the word 'mithl' indicates sharing in some attributes but not full identity, creating doubt. The commentary from al-'Inaya says this ruling applies regardless of intention, while al-Mabsut considers intention to remove doubt, but the relied-upon position is that intention does not effect freedom. (2) 'Ma anta illa al-hurr' — the slave is freed because it is an emphatic affirmation, like the Shahada. (3) 'Ra'suka ra'su hurr' (with idafa) — the slave is not freed because it is a simile with the particle of comparison omitted; the original meaning is 'your head is like the head of a free person', which is a simile like the first case. (4) 'Ra'suka ra'sun hurr' (descriptive compound, tawsifi) — the slave is freed because it directly attributes freedom to the head, and the head is a term that can represent the whole body. The passage notes that this principle is also applied in divorce law.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl): A word signifying likeness; when used, it indicates sharing in some attributes, not full identity.
استثناء من النفی (istithna min al-nafi): Exception after negation, which results in emphatic affirmation.
تشبیہ بحذف حرف (tashbih bi-hadf harf): Simile with omission of the particle, e.g., 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' meaning 'your head is like the head of a free person'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (with idafa), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave becomes free because the head represents the whole body.
B) The slave does not become free because it is a simile with omission of the particle.
C) The slave becomes free only if the master intends emancipation.
D) The slave does not become free because the head is not a complete body.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic precision is crucial in legal utterances: the Hanafi school distinguishes between simile (which creates doubt) and emphatic affirmation (which establishes certainty) to determine whether emancipation occurs.

Linguistic Precision in Slave Emancipation: Four Expressions
Rulings on four expression types and their effect on freeing a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the four expressions implying freedom and explain the linguistic reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
A slave is freed only when the master's words remove all doubt; simile and analogy create doubt, while emphatic assertion removes it.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a master's statement determines whether his slave is emancipated. This lesson breaks down four common expressions and explains why some free the slave and others do not. By analyzing the underlying linguistic principles, you will see how doubt (shakk) and emphasis (ta'kid) drive the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four expressions
Locate the four statements the master might make: (1) 'anta mithl al-hurr' (you are like a free person), (2) 'ma anta illa al-hurr' (you are nothing but free), (3) 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (your head is the head of a free person, with idafa), (4) 'ra'suka ra'sun hurr' (your head is a free head, with tawsif).
The difference between (3) and (4) lies in the grammatical construction: idafa (genitive construction) vs. tawsif (descriptive phrase).
2
Classify each expression linguistically
Determine the type of speech: (1) simile with the word 'mithl', (2) exception from negation, (3) simile without the particle 'ka', (4) direct attribution of freedom to a body part.
Expression (2) uses 'istithna min al-nafi', which Hanafis treat as emphatic affirmation.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: doubt prevents emancipation
If the expression creates doubt about whether the master intended full freedom, emancipation does not occur. Simile (1) and (3) imply similarity, not identity, thus doubt remains. Expression (4) directly attributes freedom to the head, which represents the whole body, so no doubt.
For expression (1), some Hanafi authorities (al-Mabsut) say intention can remove doubt if the master intended emancipation.
4
Determine the ruling for each expression
Based on the principle: (1) slave not freed (with nuance on intention), (2) slave is freed, (3) slave not freed, (4) slave is freed.
Expression (2) is analogous to the shahada: 'la ilaha illa Allah' is emphatic affirmation; similarly 'ma anta illa al-hurr' emphatically affirms freedom.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For expression (1), the Hidayah states an absolute ruling that the slave is not freed, but al-Mabsut records that if the master intended emancipation, the slave is freed. The mufta bihi position is that without intention, it does not occur; with intention, it does.
This nuance highlights the role of intention (niyyah) in declarations of emancipation.

واذا قال لعبده انت مثل الحر لم يعتق لان المثل يستعمل للمشاركة في بعض المعنى عرفا فوقع الشک فی الحرية ولوقال ما انت الاحر عتق لان الاستثناء من النفى اثبات على وجه التاكيد كما في كلمة الشهادة و لوقال راسک راسَ حرلا يعتق لانه تشبيه بحذف حرفه ولوقال راسک راس حرعتق لانه اثبات الحرية فيه اذ الرأس يعبر به عن جميع البدن

And if he says to his slave 'You are like a free person', he is not freed because the word 'like' is used customarily for sharing in some meaning, so doubt occurs in freedom. And if he says 'You are nothing but free', he is freed because exception from negation is affirmation in a way of emphasis, as in the word of testimony. And if he says 'Your head is the head of a free person' (with idafa), he is not freed because it is a simile by dropping its particle. And if he says 'Your head is a free head' (with tawsifi), he is freed because it is establishing freedom in it, since the head is used to express the entire body.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage states four rulings regarding a master's statements to his slave. The first three are: (1) 'You are like a free person' does not emancipate because 'like' implies sharing in some attributes, not complete identity, creating doubt. (2) 'You are nothing but free' does emancipate because the exception from negation affirms the positive emphatically, analogous to the shahada. (3) 'Your head is the head of a free person' (with idafa) does not emancipate because it is a simile without the particle 'ka', so it is like the first case. (4) 'Your head is a free head' (with tawsifi) does emancipate because it attributes freedom to the head, and the head represents the whole body. The commentary adds that in the first case, some scholars say intention matters: if the master intended emancipation, it occurs, resolving the doubt.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl): In this context, 'like' or 'similar' used for sharing in some attributes, not complete identity.
استثناء (istithna'): Exception from a negation, which affirms the positive emphatically.
تشبیہ (tashbih): Simile or comparison, typically requiring a particle like 'ka'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the word 'hurr' is not explicitly stated.
B) Because it is a simile without the particle 'ka', which is weaker and creates doubt.
C) Because the head cannot represent the whole body.
D) Because the master did not intend freedom.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the precise wording and linguistic structure of a master's statement determine whether a slave is freed; simile and doubt prevent emancipation, while emphatic affirmation and direct attribution of freedom to the whole body effect emancipation.

Automatic Emancipation of a Mahram Relative: Hanafi Rule and Shafi'i Counterargument
inter_madhhab_dispute with intra-Hanafi sub-issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that owning a permanent mahram relative triggers automatic emancipation, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection based on the effective cause (illa) and the role of hadith.

Core Idea
Owning a mahram relative (even non-blood) frees him automatically — the illa is permanent mahramiyya, not bloodline.

The Hanafi school holds that whoever becomes owner of a relative who is a permanent mahram (e.g., brother, uncle, foster brother) must see that slave freed immediately, even if the owner does not wish it. This ruling is based on a prophetic hadith and the broader principle that mahramiyya creates a bond so strong that ownership cannot override it. However, Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees when the relation is not by birth (e.g., foster brother), relying on qiyas. In this lesson, we unpack the textual proof, the Hanafi definition of the effective cause, and the practical consequences, including a sub-issue about a mukatab (slave under contract of kitabah) buying his brother.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its textual basis
State the rule: if a person owns a dhi rahm mahram relative (a relative permanently forbidden in marriage), that slave is freed automatically. Locate the hadith quoted in the source: 'Whoever owns a dhi rahm mahram, he is free.' Note that the wording is general ('amm).
The hadith is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) and is considered a sound basis for the ruling.
2
Determine the effective cause (illa) in Hanafi thought
The Hanafi argument is that the effective cause ('illa) is permanent mahramiyya, not the method of establishing kinship (birth vs. fosterage). The text emphasizes that mahramiyya is the relationship whose joining is obligatory and severing is prohibited, leading to maintenance obligation and marriage prohibition. Therefore, the cause is present in both blood and foster mahrams.
Birth (wilada) is considered irrelevant (mulgha) as an effective cause; only mahramiyya matters.
3
Contrast the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts automatic emancipation to blood relations (wilada) only. His proof: qiyas argues that emancipation against the owner's will is not permitted in base cases; brotherhood through fosterage is inferior to blood brotherhood, so analogy (qiyas) prevents extending the ruling to non-blood mahrams. Also, in the context of kitabah, a mukatab does not become mukatib over a non-blood relative, indicating that non-blood relationships do not carry the same effect.
Shafi'i considers qiyas as a valid tool to limit the generality of the hadith, while Hanafis prioritize the apparent generality.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the hadith is explicit and general ('amm), and a text cannot be overridden by qiyas. The effective cause (illa) is mahramiyya, which is present in both blood and non-blood relations. The effects of mahramiyya (maintenance, prohibition of marriage) apply equally to both. Therefore, the ruling includes non-blood mahrams.
The Hanafi position is strengthened by the principle that 'al-'illa al-muaththira' (effective cause) is determined by shared attributes, not arbitrary categories.
5
Apply to the mukatab sub-issue
If a mukatab (a slave who has contracted kitabah) buys his brother (or similar mahram relative), does the brother become free? The text presents a difference: Abu Hanifa initially held that the mukatab becomes mukatib over the brother (i.e., the brother enters kitabah), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the brother is freed immediately. The preferred (mufta bihi) view is the latter: the brother is freed directly, because the mukatab lacks complete ownership that would allow him to make the brother a mukatab, and emancipation is the objective of kitabah.
The text says 'lana an namna' (we have the choice to prevent), indicating the school's adoption of the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text

من ملک ذا بمرحم محرم من متفق علیہ
فصل و من ملک دارحم محرم منه عتق عليه وهذا اللفظ مروى عن النبي عليه السلام و قال عليه السلام من اللفظ ملک دارحم محرم منه فهو حرو بعمومه ينتظم كل قرابة مؤيدة بالمحرمية ولادا او غيره والشافعي يخالفنافى غيره له ان ثبوت العتق من غير مرضاة المالك ينقيه القياس اولا يقتضيه والاخوة ومايضا هيها نازلة عن قرابة الـولا دفامتنع الالحاق والاستدلال ولهذا امتنع التكاتب على المكاتب في غير الولا دو لم يمتنع فيه ولنا ماروينا ولانه ملك قريبه قرابة مؤثرة فى المحرمية فيعتق عليه و هذا هو المؤثر في الاصل و الولا دملغى لا نهاهي التي يفترض وصلها و يحرم قطعها حتى و جبت النفقة وحرم النكاح ولا فرق بينهما اذا كان المالک مسلما او كافرافى دار الاسلام لعموم العلة والمكاتب اذا اشترى اخاه و من يجرى مجراه لا يتكاتب عليه لانه ليس له ملک تام يقدره على الاعتاق والافتراض عند القدرة بخلاف الولادلان العتق فيه من مقاصد الكتابة فامتنع البيع فيعتق تحقيق المقصود العقد عن أبي حنيفة انه يتكاتب على الاخ ايضا وهو قولهما فلنا ان نمنع وهذا بخلاف ماذا ملك ابنة عمه وهى اخته من الرضاع لان المحرمية ماثبت بالقرابة والصبى جعل اهلا لهذا العتق وكذا المجنون حتى عتق القريب عليهما عند الملک لانه تعلق به حق العبد فشابه النفقة
ترجمہ اور جوشخص اپنے ذی رحم محر کا مالک ہوتو وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گالور یہ لفظ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے کہ جو شخص کسی ذی رحم محرم کا مالک ہو تو وہ آزاد ہے اور (یہ) اپنے عموم کی وجہ سے ہر ایسے قرابتی کو شامل ہوگا جس کی محرمیت ابدی اور دائی ہو۔ (خواہ ) بطریق ولادت ہو یا دوسرے طریقہ سے ہو اور امام شافعی غیر ولاد میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مالک کی مرضی کے بغیر آزاد ہو جانے کی قیاس نفی کرتا ہے۔ یا قیاس اس کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا ہے، اور بھائی ہونے اور اس کے مانند ( کی قرابت ) قرابت ولاد سے کمتر ہے۔ پس لاحق کرنا اور استدلال کرنا منع ہو گیا اور اسی وجہ سے غیر ولاد میں مکاتب پر تکاتب ممتنع ہے اور قرابت ولاد میں ( یہ بات ) ممتنع نہیں ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل وہ حدیث ہے جو ہم نے روایت کی ہے اور اسلئے کہ وہ اپنے اپنے قریبی کا مالک ہوا جسن کی قرابت دائمی محرمیت میں مؤثر ہے۔ تو وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گا اور دراصل یہی مؤثر ہے اور ولادت مبلغی (غیر موثر) ہے۔ اسلئے قرابت محرمیت ہی ایسی چیز ہے جس کا ملا نا فرض ہے اور اس کا توڑنا حرام ہے ۔ حتی کہ نفقہ واجب ہوتا اور نکاح حرام ہوتا ہے اور ان دونوں صورتوں میں کچھ فرق نہیں ہے کہ مالک مکان مسلمان ہو یا دارالاسلام میں کا فر ہو۔ کیونکہ علت عام ہے اور مکاتب نے اگر اپنے بھائی کو یا اس کے مانند ( چا و ماموں وغیرہ) کو خریدا تو وہ مکاتب پر مکاتب نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ مکاتب کے واسطے ملک نام نہیں ہے۔ جو اس کو آزاد کرنے پر قدرت دے دے اور (مسئلہ ) قدرت کے وقت مفروض ہے۔ بخلاف ولادت کے ، کیونکہ کتابت کے مقاصد میں سے اس کی آزادی بھی ہے۔ لہذا بیع ممتنع ہے۔ پس وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ تاکہ عقد کتابت کا مقصود ثابت ہو اور ابوحنیفہ سے یہ بھی روایت ہے کہ مکاتب پر اس کا بھائی بھی مکاتب ہو جاتا ہے اور یہی صاحبین کا قول ہے تو ہم کو یہ بھی اختیار ہے کہ ہم ( بھائی کے مکاتب نہ ہو جانے کو منع کریں اور یہ بر خلاف اس صورت
Whoever owns a relative who is a permanent mahram, that slave is freed upon him. This wording is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns a dhur rahm mahram, he is free.' By its generality, it includes every relationship confirmed by permanent mahramiyya, whether by birth or otherwise. Al-Shafi'i disagrees with us regarding non-birth relationships. His proof: The establishment of freedom without the owner's consent is negated by qiyas, or qiyas does not require it. Brotherhood and similar are lower than birth relationship, so analogy and deduction are prevented. Therefore, in non-birth relationships, a mukatab is not subject to kitabah for his relative, while in birth relationships it is not prevented. Our proof: what we have narrated and because he owned his relative whose relationship is effective in mahramiyya, so he is freed upon him. This is the effective cause in principle, and birth is ignored. Because mahramiyya is the relationship whose joining is obligatory and severing is prohibited, such that maintenance is obligatory and marriage is prohibited. There is no difference whether the owner is Muslim or a non-Muslim in Dar al-Islam due to the generality of the cause. If a mukatab buys his brother or the like, he does not become a mukatib over him because he does not have complete ownership to enable emancipation, and it is presumed upon ability. Unlike birth, because emancipation is among the objectives of kitabah, so sale is prevented, and he is freed to achieve the purpose of the contract. From Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that he does become a mukatib over the brother as well, and that is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). So we have the choice to prevent (the latter) and this is contrary to the case of one who owns his cousin who is his foster sister, because mahramiyya is established by lineage, and the minor is made eligible for this emancipation, similarly the insane, so that the relative is freed upon them at the time of ownership because the slave's right is attached, resembling maintenance.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rule that owning a mahram relative (e.g., brother, uncle, etc.) automatically results in emancipation. The proof is a hadith. The effective cause ('illa) is the permanent mahramiyya relationship. Imam Shafi'i limits this to blood relatives (wilada) and excludes non-blood mahrams like foster relationships. The Hanafi response shows that mahramiyya is the effective cause, not bloodline. The text then addresses a sub-issue: if a mukatab (a slave who has contracted kitabah) buys his brother, does the brother become free? The default Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is yes, based on the objectives of kitabah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ. There is also a discussion of minors and insane persons owning such relatives, where emancipation still occurs because the slave's right is attached.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A relative who is permanently forbidden in marriage, such as brother, uncle, father, etc. Includes blood and foster relations.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave earns money to purchase freedom in installments.
علة (illa): The effective cause or legal reason behind a ruling; here it is permanent mahramiyya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person owns his foster brother (through breastfeeding), what is the ruling?
A) The foster brother is not freed because he is not a blood relative
B) The foster brother is freed automatically because he is a mahram
C) The foster brother is freed only if the owner intends it
D) The foster brother becomes free only after the owner's death

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the automatic emancipation of a mahram slave is based on the effective cause of permanent mahramiyya, not bloodline; and on the mukatab sub-issue, the preferred view is that the brother is freed immediately.

8 min
Automatic Emancipation of Mahram Relatives: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling, proof, and scope of automatic emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on automatic emancipation upon ownership of a mahram relative and explain the hadith evidence and the illa of mahramiyyah.

Core Idea
Owning a perpetual mahram relative instantly frees them based on a general hadith and the common cause of perpetual marital prohibition.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi rule that when a person acquires ownership of a relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden (mahram), that relative becomes free immediately, even without the owner's consent. The ruling is derived from a hadith and applied broadly to both lineal and non-lineal relatives as long as the mahramiyyah is perpetual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is owning (milkiyyah) a dhi rahim mahram relative. The ruling is that the relative becomes free (hurr) automatically upon ownership, regardless of the owner's intention.
This is one of several involuntary causes of emancipation in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, he is free' is quoted from the Prophet (peace be upon him). Note that it is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalala) regarding the ruling.
The hadith is narrated by al-Nasa'i and others.
3
Analyze the generality and the effective cause
The word 'man' (whoever) is general, covering all cases of mahramiyyah. The effective cause ('illa) is the existence of perpetual mahramiyyah, which applies to both lineal (wiladah) and non-lineal relations as long as the marital prohibition is permanent.
The text states that lineage alone (wiladah) is not the cause; rather, mahramiyyah is the cause.
4
Confirm the scope
The ruling applies to all owners, Muslim or non-Muslim in Dar al-Islam, and also to children and insane persons, as the right of the slave is attached.
This is based on the generality of the illa.

من ملک ذا بمرحم محرم من متفق علیہ [؟]
فصل و من ملک ذا رحم محرم منه عتق عليه وهذا اللفظ مروى عن النبي عليه السلام و قال عليه السلام من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه فهو حر بعمومه ينتظم كل قرابة مؤيدة بالمحرمية ولادا او غيره والشافعي يخالفنا فى غيره له ان ثبوت العتق من غير مرضاة المالك ينقيه القياس اولا يقتضيه والاخوة ومايضا هيها نازلة عن قرابة الـولا دفامتنع الالحاق والاستدلال ولهذا امتنع التكاتب على المكاتب في غير الولا دو لم يمتنع فيه ولنا ماروينا ولانه ملك قريبه قرابة مؤثرة فى المحرمية فيعتق عليه و هذا هو المؤثر في الاصل و الولا دملغى لا نهاهي التي يفترض وصلها و يحرم قطعها حتى و جبت النفقة وحرم النكاح ولا فرق بينهما اذا كان المالک مسلما او کافرافى دار الاسلام لعموم العلة والمكاتب اذا اشترى اخاه و من يجرى مجراه لا يتكاتب عليه لانه ليس له ملک تام يقدره على الاعتاق والافتراض عند القدرة بخلاف الولادلان العتق فيه من مقاصد الكتابة فامتنع البيع فيعتق تحقيق مقصود العقد عن أبي حنيفة انه يتكاتب على الاخ ايضا وهو قولهما فلنا ان نمنع وهذا بخلاف ماذا ملك ابنة عمه وهى اخته من الرضاع لان المحرمية ماثبت بالقرابة والصبى جعل اهلا لهذا العتق وكذا المجنون حتى عتق القريب عليهما عند الملک لانه تعلق به حق العبد فشابه النفقة

ترجمہ: اور جوشخص اپنے ذی رحم محر کا مالک ہوتو وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور یہ لفظ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے کہ جو شخص کسی ذی رحم محرم کا مالک ہو تو وہ آزاد ہے اور (یہ) اپنے عموم کی وجہ سے ہر ایسے قرابتی کو شامل ہوگا جس کی محرمیت ابدی اور دائی ہو۔ (خواہ ) بطریق ولادت ہو یا دوسرے طریقہ سے ہو اور امام شافعی غیر ولاد میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مالک کی مرضی کے بغیر آزاد ہو جانے کی قیاس نفی کرتا ہے۔ یا قیاس اس کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا ہے، اور بھائی ہونے اور اس کے مانند ( کی قرابت ) قرابت ولاد سے کمتر ہے۔ پس لاحق کرنا اور استدلال کرنا منع ہو گیا اور اسی وجہ سے غیر ولاد میں مکاتب پر تکاتب ممتنع ہے اور قرابت ولاد میں ( یہ بات ) ممتنع نہیں ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل وہ حدیث ہے جو ہم نے روایت کی ہے اور اسلئے کہ وہ اپنے اپنے قریبی کا مالک ہوا جسن کی قرابت دائمی محرمیت میں مؤثر ہے۔ تو وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گا اور دراصل یہی مؤثر ہے اور ولادت مبلغی (غیر موثر) ہے۔ اسلئے قرابت محرمیت ہی ایسی چیز ہے جس کا ملا نا فرض ہے اور اس کا توڑنا حرام ہے ۔ حتی کہ نفقہ واجب ہوتا اور نکاح حرام ہوتا ہے اور ان دونوں صورتوں میں کچھ فرق نہیں ہے کہ مالک مکان مسلمان ہو یا دارالاسلام میں کا فر ہو۔ کیونکہ علت عام ہے اور مکاتب نے اگر اپنے بھائی کو یا اس کے مانند ( چا و ماموں وغیرہ) کو خریدا تو وہ مکاتب پر مکاتب نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ مکاتب کے واسطے ملک نام نہیں ہے۔ جو اس کو آزاد کرنے پر قدرت دے دے اور (مسئلہ ) قدرت کے وقت مفروض ہے۔ بخلاف ولادت کے ، کیونکہ کتابت کے مقاصد میں سے اس کی آزادی بھی ہے۔ لہذا بیع ممتنع ہے۔ پس وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ تاکہ عقد کتابت کا مقصود ثابت ہو اور ابوحنیفہ سے یہ بھی روایت ہے کہ مکاتب پر اس کا بھائی بھی مکاتب ہو جاتا ہے اور یہی صاحبین کا قول ہے تو ہم کو یہ بھی اختیار ہے کہ ہم ( بھائی کے مکاتب نہ ہو جانے کو منع کریں اور یہ بر خلاف اس صورت کے کہ جب آدمی اپنی پھوپھی کی بیٹی کا مالک ہوا۔ حالانکہ وہ اس کی رضاعی بہن ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ محرمیت " ابت کی وجہ سے ثابت نہیں ہے اور بچہ کو بھی اس آزادی کا اہل قرار دیا گیا ہے اور یہ مجنوں کا حکم ہے حتی کہ قریبی مشخص ان دونوں پرانے مالک ہوتے ہی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس آزادی کے ساتھ بندے کا حق متعلق ہو گیا ہے۔

تشریح: مصنف نے سابق میں اعتناق اختیاری کو بیان فرمایا ہے۔ اب اس فصل میں اس اعتماق کو بیان کریں گے جو بغیر اختیار کے ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ جیسے کسی قرابتی کو خریدنا اور وہ غلام جو مسلمان ہو کر دار الحرب سے کیا ہے اس کو اصحاب سنن اربعہ نے حضرت سمرہ سے روایت کیا ہے۔ حدیث میں دو لفظ رحم اور محرم قابل ذکر ہیں۔ رحم در حقیقت ماں کے پیٹ میں بچہ کے واسطے ظرف ہوتا ہے۔ پھر بہت ولا دہ قرابت اور تعلق کا نام رحم رکھا گیا اور اس سے ذوالرحم ہے اور محرم یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک مرد ہو اور دوسری عورت تو ان کے درمیان نکاح جائز نہ ہو۔ ( عنایہ ) حدیث کے الفاظ عام ہیں جو ہر ایسے قرابتدار کو شامل ہیں جس کے ساتھ دائمی حرمت ہو۔ خواہ ولادت کے طریقہ پر ہو یا بغیر ولادت کے طریقہ سے ہو۔ ولادت کی صورت یہ ہے کہ بیٹا یا بیٹی اپنے والدین یا دادا دادی کی مالک ہوئی یا یہ لوگ اپنے بیٹے ، بیٹی یا پوتے پوتی کے مالک ہوئے اور غیر ولا د کی صورت یہ ہے کہ آدمی اپنے بھائی ، بہن یا ان کی اولاد کا مالک ہو ایا اس کے برعکس ہو۔ حضرت امام شافعی قرابت غیر ولادت میں ہمارے خلاف ہیں۔ یعنی اگر ایسے قریبی محرم کا مالک ہو، جس سے ولادت کی قرابت نہیں ہے۔ تو وہ امام شافعی کے نزدیک آزاد نہیں ہوتا اور ہمارے نزدیک آزاد ہو جاتا ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مالک کی مرضی کے بغیر آزادی کا ثابت ہونا خلاف قیاس ہے یا مقتضی قیاس کے خلاف ہے اور جو چیز خلاف قیاس ہوتی ہے۔ اس پر دوسری چیز کو قیاس نہیں کیا جاتا اور اسی طرح جو چیز مقتضی قیاس کے خلاف ہو اس کے ساتھ دوسری چیز اس وقت لاحق کی جاسکتی ہے۔ جبکہ ملحق من کل وجه ملحق بہ کے ہم معنی ہو ۔ حالانکہ یہاں ایسا نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ بھائی ہونے کی یا اس کے مانند چا اور ماموں ہونے کی قرابت، قرابت ولادت سے ادنی اور کمتر ہے۔ پس اس عدم مساوات کی وجہ سے قرابت غیر ولا دیعنی بھائی وغیرہ ہونے کی قرابت کو قرابت ولادت کے ساتھ لاحق کرنا یا دلالت انص کے ذریعہ استدلال کرنا منع ہے۔ اس فرق کی وجہ سے اگر مکاتب نے اپنے بھائی یا اس کے مانند کسی غیر ولادی قرابتدار کو خریدا یا کسی اور وجہ سے مالک ہو گیا تو وہ اس کا مکاتب نہیں ہوتا۔ حالانکہ اگر مکاتب اپنے باپ یا بیٹے کا مالک ہو جائے تو وہ بھی مکاتب ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ قرابت غیر ولا د کو قرابت ولاد پر قیاس کرنا منع ہے۔ ہماری دلیل اول تو وہ حدیث ہے جو ہم نے روایت کی ہے یعنی من ملک زار تم محرم منہ عشق علیہ چونکہ یہ حدیث مطلق ہے۔ اسلئے قرابت ولا داور قرابت غیر و لا د دونوں کو شامل ہوگی۔
Chapter on Emancipation. Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, that relative is freed upon him. This wording is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, he is free.' By its generality, it encompasses every kinship confirmed by perpetual mahramiyyah, whether by lineage or otherwise. Al-Shafi'i opposes us regarding non-lineal relatives. His proof: the establishment of emancipation without the master's consent is negated by qiyas, or qiyas does not require it. Brotherhood and similar [relations] are lower than lineal kinship, so annexation and inference are prevented. For this reason, the mukatab is not covered on [the purchase of a brother] in non-lineal [kinship], while [coverage] is not prevented in lineal kinship. Our proof: the hadith we narrated, and because he owned his relative through a kinship that is effective in mahramiyyah, so he is freed upon him. This is the effective [cause] in principle, and lineage is disregarded. It (the mahram relationship) is that which joining is obligatory and severing is forbidden, such that maintenance is obligatory and marriage is forbidden. And there is no difference between these two cases whether the owner is Muslim or a disbeliever in Dar al-Islam, due to the generality of the effective cause. If a mukatab purchases his brother or one similar to him, he does not become covered on that [slave] because he does not have complete ownership enabling him to emancipate, and the assumption [of freedom] is at the time of ability, unlike lineage, because emancipation in it is among the objectives of the kitabah. So sale is prevented, and he is freed to achieve the purpose of the contract. From Abu Hanifa: he becomes covered on the brother also, and this is the saying of the two [Sahibayn]. We have the option to prevent this. And this is unlike if one owns his paternal uncle's daughter who is his sister through suckling, because mahramiyyah is not established here by kinship. The child is made capable of this emancipation, and similarly the insane, such that the relative is freed upon them at the time of ownership, because the right of the slave attaches to it, thus resembling maintenance.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the automatic emancipation of a mahram relative upon ownership, with additional rulings on the mukatab and other details. The Hanafi school holds that when a person owns any relative who is a perpetual mahram (i.e., marriage between them is forever forbidden), that relative becomes free immediately, regardless of the owner's intention. This is based on the hadith 'Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, he is free.' The word 'dhi rahim mahram' includes both lineal descendants (e.g., parents, children) and non-lineal relations (e.g., siblings, uncles, aunts) as long as the mahramiyyah is perpetual. The hadith is general ('amm) and applies to all such cases. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees specifically for non-lineal relatives (ghayr wiladah), arguing that emancipation without the owner's consent is against qiyas (analogy) since the rule for lineal relatives is exceptional and cannot be extended to weaker relations. The Hanafi response is that the hadith's generality is authoritative, and the effective cause ('illa) for emancipation is the existence of perpetual mahramiyyah, which is common to both lineal and non-lineal cases. The text then discusses the case of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). If a mukatab purchases his brother (a non-lineal mahram), he does not become his owner's kitabah slave (i.e., the brother does not become a mukatab under him) because the mukatab's ownership is incomplete. However, if the mukatab purchases a lineal relative, that relative becomes a mukatab as well, because emancipation is a goal of the kitabah contract. There is a internal Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa has a narration that the brother also becomes a mukatab (coverage), and this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two Sahibayn). But the text says 'we have the option to prevent' (lana an namna'), implying the preferred position is that it does not occur. The passage concludes by noting that a child or an insane person is capable of causing this automatic emancipation, as the right of the slave is attached, similar to the obligation of maintenance.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden (mahram) due to blood relation (nasab), milk-suckling (rada'), or marriage (musaharah).
ولادة (wiladah): Lineal descent relationship, such as parent-child, grandparent-grandchild.
محرمية (mahramiyyah): The state of being a mahram, i.e., marriage is permanently forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is the effective cause ('illa) for automatic emancipation upon owning a relative?
A) Lineal descent (wiladah)
B) Perpetual mahramiyyah
C) The owner's intention to free the slave
D) The slave's request for freedom

Key Takeaway
Ownership of any perpetual mahram relative triggers immediate emancipation, based on the general hadith and the illa of mahramiyyah.
