Dispute on Non-Lineal Mahram Relatives and the Mukatab Case
Inter-madhhab dispute and mukatab coverage intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Shafi'i objection to automatic emancipation for non-lineal mahram relatives and evaluate the Hanafi response, including the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the mukatab.

Core Idea
The Shafi'i school restricts automatic emancipation to lineal relatives; the Hanafi school extends it to all mahram relatives, and the mukatab's incomplete ownership creates a nuanced intra-madhhab disagreement.

This lesson delves into the inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether ownership of a non-lineal mahram relative (e.g., brother, uncle) triggers automatic emancipation. The Shafi'i school argues by qiyas that non-lineal relatives are not included, while the Hanafi school relies on the generality of the hadith and the illa of mahramiyyah. The lesson also examines the special case of a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) purchasing his brother, where an internal Hanafi disagreement arises.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that emancipation without the owner's consent is against qiyas, and non-lineal kinship is weaker than lineal, so the rule cannot be extended.
The Shafi'i view relies on the principle that exceptions to qiyas should not be analogized further.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds with the hadith 'man malaka...' which is general, and the illa of mahramiyyah is common to both lineal and non-lineal cases. The hadith overrides qiyas.
The Hanafi school considers the hadith as a clear text (nass) that establishes the rule.
3
Examine the mukatab case
When a mukatab purchases his brother (non-lineal), the text says he does not become a mukatab (coverage) because the mukatab lacks complete ownership. However, if the mukatab purchases a lineal relative, coverage occurs because emancipation is a goal of kitabah.
The distinction hinges on the completeness of ownership and the purpose of the kitabah contract.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa (in one narration) and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that coverage does occur for the brother. The text states 'lana an namna' (we have the option to prevent), indicating the preferred view is no coverage.
The mufta bihi is that the brother does not become a mukatab.

ہے یعنی من ملک زار تم محرم منہ عشق علیہ چونکہ یہ حدیث مطلق ہے۔ اسلئے قرابت ولا داور قرابت غیر و لا د دونوں کو شامل ہوگی۔
Chapter on Emancipation. Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, that relative is freed upon him. This wording is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, he is free.' By its generality, it encompasses every kinship confirmed by perpetual mahramiyyah, whether by lineage or otherwise. Al-Shafi'i opposes us regarding non-lineal relatives. His proof: the establishment of emancipation without the master's consent is negated by qiyas, or qiyas does not require it. Brotherhood and similar [relations] are lower than lineal kinship, so annexation and inference are prevented. For this reason, the mukatab is not covered on [the purchase of a brother] in non-lineal [kinship], while [coverage] is not prevented in lineal kinship. Our proof: the hadith we narrated, and because he owned his relative through a kinship that is effective in mahramiyyah, so he is freed upon him. This is the effective [cause] in principle, and lineage is disregarded. It (the mahram relationship) is that which joining is obligatory and severing is forbidden, such that maintenance is obligatory and marriage is forbidden. And there is no difference between these two cases whether the owner is Muslim or a disbeliever in Dar al-Islam, due to the generality of the effective cause. If a mukatab purchases his brother or one similar to him, he does not become covered on that [slave] because he does not have complete ownership enabling him to emancipate, and the assumption [of freedom] is at the time of ability, unlike lineage, because emancipation in it is among the objectives of the kitabah. So sale is prevented, and he is freed to achieve the purpose of the contract. From Abu Hanifa: he becomes covered on the brother also, and this is the saying of the two [Sahibayn]. We have the option to prevent this. And this is unlike if one owns his paternal uncle's daughter who is his sister through suckling, because mahramiyyah is not established here by kinship. The child is made capable of this emancipation, and similarly the insane, such that the relative is freed upon them at the time of ownership, because the right of the slave attaches to it, thus resembling maintenance.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the automatic emancipation of a mahram relative upon ownership, with additional rulings on the mukatab and other details. The Hanafi school holds that when a person owns any relative who is a perpetual mahram (i.e., marriage between them is forever forbidden), that relative becomes free immediately, regardless of the owner's intention. This is based on the hadith 'Whoever owns a dhi rahim mahram relative, he is free.' The word 'dhi rahim mahram' includes both lineal descendants (e.g., parents, children) and non-lineal relations (e.g., siblings, uncles, aunts) as long as the mahramiyyah is perpetual. The hadith is general ('amm) and applies to all such cases. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees specifically for non-lineal relatives (ghayr wiladah), arguing that emancipation without the owner's consent is against qiyas (analogy) since the rule for lineal relatives is exceptional and cannot be extended to weaker relations. The Hanafi response is that the hadith's generality is authoritative, and the effective cause ('illa) for emancipation is the existence of perpetual mahramiyyah, which is common to both lineal and non-lineal cases. The text then discusses the case of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). If a mukatab purchases his brother (a non-lineal mahram), he does not become his owner's kitabah slave (i.e., the brother does not become a mukatab under him) because the mukatab's ownership is incomplete. However, if the mukatab purchases a lineal relative, that relative becomes a mukatab as well, because emancipation is a goal of the kitabah contract. There is a internal Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa has a narration that the brother also becomes a mukatab (coverage), and this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two Sahibayn). But the text says 'we have the option to prevent' (lana an namna'), implying the preferred position is that it does not occur. The passage concludes by noting that a child or an insane person is capable of causing this automatic emancipation, as the right of the slave is attached, similar to the obligation of maintenance.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.
محرمية (mahramiyyah): The state of being a mahram, i.e., marriage is permanently forbidden.
ولادة (wiladah): Lineal descent relationship, such as parent-child, grandparent-grandchild.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative position) in the Hanafi school, when a mukatab purchases his brother (non-lineal mahram), what happens to the brother?
A) He becomes free immediately.
B) He becomes a mukatab under his brother (coverage).
C) He remains a slave, no change.
D) He becomes free only after the mukatab completes his kitabah payments.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes all perpetual mahram relatives in automatic emancipation, overriding Shafi'i qiyas, and the mukatab's incomplete ownership prevents coverage for non-lineal relatives.

intermediate
8 min
Ownership of a Mahram Relative Leads to Emancipation: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling and proof for emancipation upon owning a mahram relative, with Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation upon owning a mahram relative, explain the Shafi'i objection, and construct the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Owning any mahram relative (lineal or non-lineal) automatically frees them—the cause is permanent marriage prohibition, not lineage.

This passage addresses a foundational dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: if a person becomes owner of a blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (mahram), does that relative become free automatically? The Hanafi school, based on a general hadith and the effective cause of mahramiyyah, extends the ruling to all mahram relatives—both lineal (parents, children) and non-lineal (siblings, uncles, aunts). Imam al-Shafi'i restricts the rule to lineal relatives only, arguing that the original ruling is an exception to qiyas and cannot be extended. In this lesson, we will dissect the proofs on both sides and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Ruling
State the Hanafi position: if a person owns a mahram relative (lineal or non-lineal), that relative becomes free automatically. Note the conditions and the underlying cause (sabab).
The ruling is a hukm wad'i (declaratory): ownership is a cause, emancipation is the effect.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the hadith quoted: 'man malaka dha rahm muharram minhu fa huwa hurr'. Understand its transmission (narrated by Ibn 'Umar and Samurah) and its textual features (general wording).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but the meaning is clear (qati' al-dalalah) according to the Hanafis.
3
Understand the Shafi'i Objection
State the Shafi'i position: only lineal relatives (parents, children) are freed. Their proof: the ruling is an exception to qiyas, so it cannot be extended by analogy. Non-lineal relatives are inferior and not equal to lineal ones.
The Shafi'is also cite the case of a mukatab purchasing his brother, where the brother does not become a mukatab.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Examine the Hanafi replies: (1) the hadith is general and not restricted to lineage; (2) the effective cause is mahramiyyah, which is present in all mahram relatives; (3) the mukatab case is not about emancipation but about the purpose of kitabah (freeing oneself and immediate family brings shame, not a brother).
Imam Abu Hanifa even holds that the brother does become a mukatab, removing the objection.
5
Determine the Practical Difference (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Identify real-world cases: e.g., a person inherits his brother as a slave or purchases his uncle. Under Hanafi law, they become free; under Shafi'i, they remain slaves.
The difference also affects awqaf and inheritance calculations.

فرض کیا گیا ہے جبکہ آزاد کرنے کی پوری قدرت ہو۔ لیکن اگر کوئی یہ اشکال کرے کہ واقعی اگر یہ بات ہے تو قرابت ولا دبھی اس پر آزاد نہ ہونا چاہئے ۔ حالانکہ وہ آزاد ہو جاتا ہے۔ تو بخلاف الولاد سے مصنف ہدایہ نے اس کا جواب دیا ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ آزادی قرابت ولاد میں مقصود کتابت ہے۔ کیونکہ جب اپنے آپ کو آزاد کرنا مقصود کتابت ہے تو قرابت ولاد کو آز دکرنا بھی مقاصد کتابت میں سے ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ انسان جس طرح اپنے مملوک ہونے سے عار اور شرم محسوس کرتا ہے۔ اسی طرح والد اور ولد کے مملوک ہونے سے بھی عار محسوس کرتا ہے۔ پس جب قرابت ولاد کا آزاد کرنا مقصود کتابت ہے، تو مکاتب نے اگر اپنے ولا دی قرابتدار باپ وغیرہ کو خریدا تو وہ بھی مکاتب ہوگا اور اس کی بیع ممتنع ہوگی اور جب مکاتب بدل کتابت ادا کر کے آزاد ہو گا تو وہ بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ تا کہ کتابت کا مقصود ثابت ہو۔ اور رہی قرابت غیر ولا و بھائی وغیرہ کی آزادی تو وہ کتابت کے مقاصد میں سے نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ جس طرح باپ اور بیٹے کے مملوک ہونے سے عار ہوتی ہے بھائی کے مملوک ہونے سے عار نہیں ہوتی۔ (عنایه ، بینی)
جواب کے شروع میں بھی ذکر کیا گیا ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایک روایت یہ بھی ہے کہ مکاتب پر اس کا بھائی بھی مکاتب ہو جاتا ہے
Chapter of Emancipation
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of al-Hidayah, volume five: When a man becomes owner of his paternal aunt's daughter, even though she is his foster-sister, because this mahramiyyah is not established by lineage, and the child is also deemed worthy of this freedom, and this is the ruling of a madman, so that even if close specified persons are the previous owners, he will become free, because the right of the slave has become attached to this freedom. Explanation: The author previously mentioned voluntary emancipation. Now in this chapter he will mention the emancipation that is established without choice, such as purchasing a relative or the slave who becomes Muslim and comes from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam. Quduri stated the ruling: If a person becomes owner of a blood relative who is a mahram to him, then that blood relative mahram will become free for him. The phrase of Quduri is narrated from the Prophet ﷺ, as he ﷺ said: "Whoever becomes owner of a blood relative who is a mahram to him, then he is free." The words of the hadith that Quduri mentioned were narrated by Imam Nasa'i from Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), and the hadith that the author of al-Hidayah mentioned with the words "fa huwa hurr" was narrated by the authors of the four Sunans from Samurah (may Allah be pleased with him). In the hadith, the two words "rahm" and "mahram" are noteworthy. "Rahm" literally is the womb in the mother, then by extension it is used for lineage and relationship, and from it is "dhu rahm". And "mahram" is that if one is a man and the other a woman, then marriage between them is not permissible. (al-'Inayah) The words of the hadith are general, including every relative with whom there is permanent prohibition, whether through lineage or without lineage. The case of lineage is that a son or daughter becomes owner of his parents or grandparents, or these become owners of their son, daughter, or grandson/granddaughter. The case of non-lineage is that a man becomes owner of his brother, sister, or their offspring, or the opposite. Imam al-Shafi'i differs from us regarding non-lineage relatives. That is, if one becomes owner of a close mahram with whom there is no lineage relation, then in the view of Imam al-Shafi'i he does not become free, whereas in our view he becomes free. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that establishing freedom without the owner's will is contrary to qiyas or is against what qiyas requires, and something contrary to qiyas cannot be analogized to something else. Similarly, something against what qiyas requires can only be attached to another if the attached is in every respect equal to that to which it is attached, but that is not the case here. Because the relationship of brotherhood or the like, such as maternal uncle, is inferior and lower than lineage relationship. Therefore, due to this inequality, it is not permissible to attach non-lineage relationship (brother etc.) to lineage relationship or to argue by dalalat al-nass. Due to this difference, if a mukatab purchases his brother or a similar non-lineage relative, or becomes owner by some other reason, then that person does not become a mukatab with him. Whereas if a mukatab becomes owner of his father or son, then that one also becomes a mukatab. So it is established that it is not permissible to analogize non-lineage relationship to lineage relationship. Our first proof is the hadith we narrated: "Whoever becomes owner of a blood relative who is a mahram to him, then he is free" (man malaka dha rahm muharram minhu fa huwa hurr). Since this hadith is absolute, it includes both lineage and non-lineage relationships.

The second proof is that when a person becomes owner of his close relative whose relationship effects permanent prohibition, then that relative becomes free for him, and in the original case (lineage) it is the mahram relationship that is effective, not lineage itself. The conclusion is that the Lawgiver (peace be upon him) considered the relationship that effects permanent prohibition. Thus he said: "Whoever becomes owner of a blood relative who is a mahram to him, then he is free" (man malaka dha rahm muharram minhu 'utuqa 'alayh). By "rahm" is meant relationship, and by "mahram" is meant prohibition of marriage. So he ﷺ said: Whoever becomes owner of a close relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, then that relative becomes free for him, whether lineage connection exists or not. Thus it is established that in lineage too, it is only the ownership of a mahram relative that is effective in establishing freedom, and lineage has no role in the occurrence of freedom. Because mahram relationship is such that maintaining it is obligatory and severing it is prohibited; even the maintenance of a mahram relative is obligatory and marriage with him/her is prohibited. The author of al-Hidayah says: In Dar al-Islam, whether the owner is Muslim or disbeliever, in both cases the owned becomes free due to ownership of a relative. Similarly, whether the owned is free or disbeliever, because the cause of mahram relationship is general. The restriction of Dar al-Islam is mentioned because if a Muslim frees his harbi slave in Dar al-Harb, he does not become free for him. Likewise, if a harbi man becomes owner of his blood relative mahram in Dar al-Harb, he does not become free for him. (al-'Inayah) And "al-mukatab idha ishtarahu akhah wa man yajri majrahu" is a response to Imam al-Shafi'i's argument, "wa li hadha imtana'a al-takattub 'ala al-mukatab fi ghayr al-wilad." The summary of the response is: Firstly, we do not accept that if a mukatab buys his brother or similar ones like paternal uncle, maternal uncle, etc., then he does not become a mukatab with him. Rather, it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that if a mukatab buys his brother or a non-lineage relative, the one who is bought also becomes a mukatab with the mukatab. And if we concede that he does not become a mukatab, the reason is that the mukatab does not have full ownership that would give him the power to free, because as long as even one dirham remains against the mukatab, he remains a slave, and a person who does not have power to free cannot have anyone freed on him. Therefore, the case is assumed when he has full power to free. But if someone objects: If that is the case, then lineage relatives should also not become free for him, yet they do become free. Then the author of al-Hidayah answers that contrary to lineage (bi khilaf al-wilad). The summary of the answer is: Freedom of lineage relatives is intended in kitabah (contract of manumission). Because when freeing oneself is the object of kitabah, then freeing lineage relatives is also among the purposes of kitabah. Just as a person feels shame and embarrassment at his own enslavement, similarly he feels shame at the enslavement of his parents and children. Thus, since freeing lineage relatives is a purpose of kitabah, if a mukatab purchases his lineage relative (father etc.), then that one also becomes a mukatab, and his sale is prevented. When the mukatab pays the kitabah price and becomes free, then that one also becomes free, so that the purpose of kitabah is achieved. As for freedom of non-lineage relatives (brother etc.), it is not among the purposes of kitabah. Because whereas shame is felt at the enslavement of father and son, no shame is felt at the enslavement of a brother. (al-'Inayah, Bini)
At the beginning of the response, it is also mentioned that there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that his brother also becomes a mukatab with him.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling on ownership of a mahram relative. The author begins by stating the established Hanafi ruling: if a person becomes the owner of any blood relative who is a mahram (i.e., permanently prohibited in marriage), that relative becomes free automatically, without any need for the owner's intention or action. This is based on a hadith narrated in multiple chains: 'Whoever becomes owner of a blood relative who is a mahram to him, then he is free.' The word 'rahm' in the hadith refers to any blood relationship, and 'mahram' means marriage is permanently prohibited between them. The hadith is general, covering both lineal relatives (parents, children, grandparents) and non-lineal relatives (brothers, sisters, uncles, aunts, etc.). The author then explains the difference between lineal and non-lineal relationships, and mentions that Imam al-Shafi'i restricts the ruling to lineal relatives only. The Shafi'i argument is that extending the ruling to non-lineal relatives is contrary to qiyas (analogical reasoning) because the original case (lineal) was established as an exception to the normal rule that ownership does not automatically free. Analogies require equality between the original and the branch case, but non-lineal relationships are inferior to lineal ones. The Hanafi response relies on the absolute wording of the hadith and the fact that the effective cause ('illa) is mahramiyyah (permanent prohibition of marriage), not the type of relationship. Both lineal and non-lineal mahrams share this cause. Furthermore, the Hanafis argue that the maintenance and prohibition of marriage apply equally to all mahrams. The author also addresses an objection from the Shafi'i side regarding the case of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) purchasing his brother. The Shafi'is claim that the fact that the brother does not become a mukatab shows that non-lineal relatives are not treated like lineal ones. The Hanafis respond that Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the brother does become a mukatab (in one narration), but even if we accept the other view, the reason is the mukatab's lack of full ownership power, not the nature of the relationship. Moreover, the purpose of the mukatab's contract is to gain freedom for himself and his immediate ascendants/descendants, as enslavement of these brings shame, which is not the case for siblings. Thus, the Hanafi position is that all mahram relatives are freed upon ownership, and this is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm muharram): A blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited. This includes both lineal (parents, children) and non-lineal (siblings, uncles, aunts, etc.) relations.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to earn and pay a specified sum in installments for his freedom. He remains partially a slave until full payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man becomes owner of his paternal uncle (who is a mahram), what is the ruling?
A) The uncle remains a slave because he is not a lineal relative.
B) The uncle becomes free automatically without any action by the owner.
C) The uncle becomes free only if the owner intends to free him.
D) The uncle becomes a mukatab automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that owning any mahram relative (lineal or non-lineal) causes automatic emancipation, based on the general hadith and the effective cause of permanent marriage prohibition.

intermediate
12 min
Ownership of a Mahram Relative: Does It Apply to a Mukatab's Purchase?
Presents second proof for mahram emancipation, then debates Shafi'i on mukatab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on automatic emancipation upon owning a mahram relative, explain the Shafi'i objection regarding the mukatab, and evaluate the Hanafi response using both textual and purposive arguments.

Core Idea
Owning a mahram relative frees them; for a mukatab, only lineage relatives are freed due to shame.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. While both agree that owning a mahram relative (e.g., mother, sister) automatically frees that relative, they differ on whether a mukatab—a slave who has contracted to buy his freedom—can cause his non-lineage mahram brother to also become a mukatab. The Hanafi school holds that he can, based on a narration from Abu Hanifa and the view of the Sahibayn, while Shafi'i argues that the mukatab's incomplete ownership prevents this. We will trace the proofs, the counterarguments, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and its evidence
Recall the hadith: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he becomes free upon him.' Identify the effective cause (illa) as the relationship of perpetual marital prohibition (hurmat al-nikah), not lineage per se.
This rule applies regardless of the owner's religion or the slave's religion, as long as the owner is in Dar al-Islam.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if a mukatab buys his brother (a non-lineage mahram), the brother does not become a mukatab because the mukatab lacks complete ownership (milk tam) required to effect emancipation.
The mukatab is part slave until he pays all installments, so his ownership is weak.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold. First, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa and the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that the brother does become a mukatab. Second, even if one accepts Shafi'i's premise, the distinction between lineage and non-lineage mahram is based on the purpose of kitabah: freeing a lineage relative (father, son) is a purpose because of shame, while freeing a brother is not.
The text also addresses an objection about foster-sisters, clarifying that only lineage-based mahram counts.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi variation
Note that Abu Hanifa has two narrations: one where the brother becomes a mukatab, and one where he does not. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position follows the Sahibayn and the stronger narration, making the brother a mukatab.
This shows that even within the school, there is some latitude.
5
Apply to edge cases
Consider cases where the owner is a child or insane: they also own the mahram relative, so emancipation occurs by the very fact of ownership, regardless of intent. Similarly, the ruling is extended by analogy to nafaqah (maintenance) obligations.
This demonstrates the legal maxim that the right of the slave is attached to the property.

ملکیت حاصل نہیں ہوتی جو اس کو آزاد کرنے پر قدرت دیدے کیونکہ مکاتب پر جب تک ایک درہم باقی ہے وہ غلام ہی رہے گا اور جو شخص آزاد کرنے کی قدرت نہیں رکھتا اس پر کوئی آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ مسئلہ اس وقت فرض کیا گیا ہے جبکہ آزاد کرنے کی پوری قدرت ہو۔ لیکن اگر کوئی یہ اشکال کرے کہ واقعی اگر یہ بات ہے تو قرابت ولاد بھی اس پر آزاد نہ ہونا چاہئے ۔ حالانکہ وہ آزاد ہو جاتا ہے۔ تو بخلاف الولاد سے مصنف ہدایہ نے اس کا جواب دیا ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ آزادی قرابت ولاد میں مقصود کتابت ہے۔ کیونکہ جب اپنے آپ کو آزاد کرنا مقصود کتابت ہے تو قرابت ولاد کو آزاد کرنا بھی مقاصد کتابت میں سے ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ انسان جس وغیرہ کو خریدا تو وہ بھی مکاتب ہوگا اور اس کی بیع ممتنع ہوگی اور جب مکاتب بدل کتابت ادا کر کے آزاد ہو گا تو وہ بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ تا کہ کتابت کا مقصود ثابت ہو۔ اور رہی قرابت غیر ولادی بھائی وغیرہ کی حنیفہ سے ایک روایت یہ بھی ہے کہ مکاتب پر اس کا بھائی بھی مکاتب ہو جاتا ہے اور یہی صاحبین کا قول ہے۔ لہذا امام شافعی نے جو امتناع بیان کیا تھا۔ ہمیں وہی تسلیم نہیں۔ کیونکہ ہم اس کو منہ نہیں جانتے ہیں۔ و هذا بخلاف ما اذا ملک ابنة عمه سے بھی ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر ذی رحم محرم کا مالک ہونا علت ہے اس کے آزاد ہونے کی اس شخص پر جو اس کا مالک ہوا ہے تو چا کی بیٹی جو اسکی رضاعی بہن بھی ہے وجہ سے ہے نہ کہ قرابت کی وجہ سے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر بچہ یا مجنون اپنے کسی قریبی محرم کا مالک ہوا تو وہ بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس آزادی کیساتھ بندے کا حق متعلق ہو گیا اور علت یعنی ذو رحم محرم کا مالک ہونا بھی پایا گیا اس لئے وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا، پس نفقہ کے مشابہ ہو گیا یعنی جیسے قریب محرم محتاج کا نفقہ بچہ اور مجنون کے مال میں واجب ہوتا ہے۔ ایسے ہی اگر یہ کسی ذو رحم محرم کے مالک ہوں تو وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
Second proof is that when a person owns a close relative whose relationship affects perpetual marital prohibition, then that relative becomes free upon him. And the principle is that in paternal lineage, only the relationship of marital prohibition is effective, and lineage has no effect. The conclusion is that the Lawgiver (peace be upon him) considered that relationship which is effective in perpetual prohibition. Thus He said: 'Whoever owns a womb-relative who is a mahram to him, he becomes free upon him.' By 'rahm' is meant kinship, and by 'mahram' is meant marital prohibition. As if the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Whoever becomes owner of such a close relative with whom marriage is perpetually forbidden, then he becomes free upon him, whether lineage relationship exists or not. So it is established that even in the case of a descendant, in establishing emancipation, owning a mahram relative is effective, and lineage has no role in the occurrence of emancipation. Because the relationship of mahram is such that connecting it is obligatory and severing it is forbidden. Even the maintenance of a mahram relative is obligatory and marriage with them is forbidden. The author of al-Hidayah says: In the Abode of Islam, whether the owner is Muslim or non-Muslim, in both cases the owned becomes free due to owning the relative. Similarly, whether the owned is free or non-Muslim, because the cause of mahram relationship is general. He mentioned the condition of the Abode of Islam because if a Muslim frees his war captive slave in the Abode of War, it does not free him. Similarly, if a warring person becomes owner of his mahram relative in the Abode of War, it does not free him. (Inayah) And 'if a mukatab buys his brother or someone of similar status' is a response to Imam Shafi'i's argument 'and for this reason, becoming a mukatab is prevented for the mukatab regarding other than lineage'. The summary of the response is: Firstly, we do not accept that if a mukatab buys his brother or similar, like maternal uncle, then the purchased one does not become a mukatab with him. Rather, it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that if a mukatab buys his brother etc., i.e., a non-lineage relative, then the one purchased also becomes a mukatab with him. And if we accept that, the reason for not becoming a mukatab is that the mukatab does not have complete ownership that would give him the power to free him, because as long as even one dirham remains upon the mukatab, he remains a slave, and one who does not have the power to free cannot have anyone freed upon him. Because the issue is assumed at the time when he has complete power to free. But if someone objects that if this is the case, then even the lineage relative should not be freed upon him, while in fact he becomes free. Then the author of al-Hidayah responded with 'contrary to lineage'. The summary of the response is that emancipation of a lineage relative is among the purposes of kitabah. Because when freeing oneself is the purpose of kitabah, then freeing a lineage relative is also among the purposes of kitabah. Just as a person feels shame and disgrace from being owned, similarly he feels shame from his father and son being owned. So when freeing a lineage relative is a purpose of kitabah, then if a mukatab buys his lineage relative like his father, then he also becomes a mukatab, and his sale is prevented, and when the mukatab pays the kitabah price and becomes free, he also becomes free, so that the purpose of kitabah is established. As for the freeing of non-lineage relatives like a brother, that is not among the purposes of kitabah, because just as shame is felt from a father and son being owned, no shame is felt from a brother being owned. (Inayah, Bayani) At the beginning of the response, it was also mentioned that there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that his brother also becomes a mukatab with the mukatab, and this is the statement of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Therefore, what Imam Shafi'i mentioned as prevention, we do not accept, because we do not know it. 'And this is unlike when he owns a cousin' is also an answer to a question. The question is: If owning a mahram relative is the cause of emancipation for the person who owned him, then a person's daughter who is also his foster-sister should become free upon her paternal cousin if he buys her, while she does not become free. The answer is that by mahram what is meant is that mahram in which lineage is effective, and this is not the case because the mahram is due to fosterage, not due to lineage. The author of al-Hidayah said: If a child or an insane person becomes owner of some mahram relative, then he also becomes free. Because the right of the servant is attached to this emancipation, and the cause i.e., owning a mahram relative is also found, so he becomes free. Thus it resembles maintenance, i.e., just as the maintenance of a needy mahram relative is obligatory in the wealth of a child and an insane person, similarly if they own a mahram relative, he becomes free. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute with supporting proofs. It begins by stating the second proof for the ruling that owning a mahram relative automatically frees that relative. The proof is a hadith: 'Whoever owns a womb-relative who is a mahram to him, he becomes free upon him.' The commentator explains that the effective cause is the relationship of perpetual marital prohibition, not lineage. This is why a child or insane person's ownership also causes emancipation, as the right of the slave is attached. Then the passage addresses a dispute with Imam Shafi'i regarding a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom). Shafi'i argued that if a mukatab buys his brother (a non-lineage mahram), the brother does not become a mukatab with him. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the brother does become a mukatab, and this is also the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Second, even if we accept Shafi'i's premise, the reason is that the mukatab lacks full ownership to effect emancipation, whereas in the case of a lineage relative (father, son), the purpose of the kitabah contract includes freeing such relatives due to the shame of their servitude. The passage also addresses an objection: if mahram ownership is the cause, why does owning a foster-sister (who is mahram due to fosterage) not cause emancipation? The answer is that the mahram considered here is only that based on lineage, not fosterage or marriage. Finally, the ruling is extended to children and the insane: since the cause (ownership of a mahram) is present, emancipation occurs, analogous to the obligation of maintenance.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A blood relative with whom marriage is perpetually forbidden due to close kinship (e.g., mother, sister, daughter, father, brother).
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
كتابت (kitabah): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave pays a sum in installments in exchange for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi relied-upon position, what happens when a mukatab buys his brother (a non-lineage mahram)?
A) The brother remains a slave and can be sold by the original master.
B) The brother becomes free immediately.
C) The brother becomes a mukatab with the mukatab.
D) The contract of kitabah becomes void.

Key Takeaway
Owning a mahram relative causes automatic emancipation; for a mukatab, this applies to non-lineage mahram brothers/uncles as well, making them mukatab, based on the hadith and purposive reasoning.

Validity of Emancipation Regardless of Intended Dedicatee
States that emancipation is valid regardless of dedicatee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on emancipation when the act is dedicated to other than Allah, and explain why the emancipation is valid despite a sinful intention.

Core Idea
The pillar (rukn) of emancipation is the utterance; intention does not affect validity.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi law: the validity of an act depends on its essential elements (arkan), not on the spiritual intention behind it. The text shows that even if a slave is emancipated for Satan or an idol, the emancipation is effective because the rukn is present. The person may be sinful, but the legal consequence stands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its required elements
What is the act being ruled upon? What are the necessary elements (rukn) for its validity? The text states that the rukn is the pronouncement of emancipation by a qualified person (the owner, who is free, sane, adult) to his slave.
The rukn is the utterance, not the intention.
2
Note the presence of the rukn in all three scenarios
In each scenario (for Allah, for Satan, for an idol), the owner utters the words of emancipation. Thus the rukn is present in all cases.
The qualifying conditions (shurut) are also present: the owner is qualified and the slave is his property.
3
Examine the effect of the dedicatee (intention) on validity
The intention to dedicate the act to a particular being does not affect the rukn. The mention of 'for Allah's sake' is an addition (ziyadah); its absence does not harm validity. The act's legal efficacy is independent of the dedicatee.
The dedicatee only affects reward (qurbah), not validity.
4
Distinguish between legal validity and spiritual reward
Even though the emancipation is valid, if the dedicatee is other than Allah (e.g., Satan or an idol), the emancipator commits a sin. The negation of reward does not negate the legal effect.
This distinction guards against the misconception that a sinful purpose invalidates the act.

ومن اعتق عبدا لوجه الله تعالى او للشيطان او للصنم عتق لوجود ركن الاعتاق من اهله في محله ووصف القربة فى اللفظ الأول زيادة فلايختل العتق بعدمه في اللفظين الآخرين
ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے اپنے غلام کو اللہ کی خوشنودی حاصل کرنے کے واسطے آزاد کیا یا شیطان کے واسطے یا بت کے واسطے تو غلام (بہر صورت) آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے کا رکن اس کے اہل سے اس کے محل میں پایا گیا اور لفظ اول میں قربت (لوجہ اللہ) کا ذکر زیادتی ہے۔ پس بعد والے دونوں لفظوں میں اس کے نہ ہونے سے خلل واقع نہیں ہوگا۔

Whoever gave a slave for Allah, gave for Satan, freed for an idol – he becomes free. And whoever frees a slave for the sake of Allah Almighty, or for Satan, or for an idol, the emancipation is valid because the pillar of emancipation exists from its qualified performer in its place, and the description of nearness (for Allah) in the first phrase is an addition, so emancipation is not defective by its absence in the other two phrases. Translation: And a person who freed his slave for the sake of obtaining Allah's pleasure, or for Satan, or for an idol, the slave becomes free in any case. Because the pillar of emancipation, i.e., the word of emancipation, was found from its qualified performer in its place, and in the first phrase, the mention of nearness (for Allah) is an addition. Therefore, its absence in the other two phrases does not cause defect. Explanation: Whoever freed his slave for Allah's sake, or for Satan, or for an idol, the slave becomes free in any case. The proof is that the pillar of emancipation, i.e., the word of emancipation, was found from its qualified performer in its place. Because the emancipator is himself free, sane, adult, and owner of the slave, and the slave is his property. And in the first case, saying 'for Allah's sake' is not necessary; rather, it is an extra thing. So in the case of Satan or idol, its absence does not harm. However, in these two cases, the emancipator will be sinful, even though the slave becomes free. Because in the case of Satan and idol, at most there is negation of nearness to Allah, but negation of nearness does not necessitate negation of emancipation.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is a direct ruling statement. The text establishes that the validity of emancipation depends on the pillar (rukn) which is the pronouncement of emancipation by a qualified person (free, adult, sane, owner) on his property. The intention or dedicatee (intended recipient of reward) does not affect the validity. Even if one says 'I free this slave for Satan' or 'for this idol', the emancipation is valid because the necessary elements are present. However, the person commits a sin by attributing the act to other than Allah. The underlying principle is that the negation of reward (qurbah) does not negate the legal effect of the act. This ruling guards against the misconception that an invalid intention or statement of dedication would nullify the emancipation.

Key Terms
ركن الاعتاق (rukn al-i'taq): The essential element of emancipation, which is the pronouncement of the word of emancipation (e.g., 'ant free') by a qualified person (owner, sane, adult, free) to his slave.
قربة (qurbah): Nearness to Allah; an act done to attain His pleasure.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation is valid even if dedicated to Satan or an idol; the rukn of pronouncement is sufficient.

Emancipation by a Coerced Person or Drunkard
States ruling that emancipation by a coerced person or drunkard is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation by a coerced person or drunkard and explain the reasoning by analogy to divorce.

Core Idea
Coercion or intoxication does not nullify emancipation if the utterance is made by a qualified person.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of certain legal acts depends on the presence of the pillar (rukn) and the capacity of the doer. This lesson examines a clear ruling: the emancipation pronounced by a person under coercion or intoxication is valid. The reasoning is analogical to divorce, where similar conditions apply. Understanding this ruling highlights the Hanafi emphasis on external expression over internal intention in some contractual acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is emancipation (itq) performed by a person who is either coerced (mukrah) or intoxicated (sakran). The ruling is that it is valid and takes effect.
2
Understand the reasoning
The Pillar (rukn) of emancipation is the utterance by a qualified person. Coercion and intoxication do not remove the qualification (ahliyya) for utterance, so the effect follows. This is analogized from divorce (talaq), where the same rule applies.
Hanafi usul: the rukn is the essential act; intention (niyya) is not a condition for validity in these cases.
3
Compare with the opposing view
Imam Shafi'i holds that intention is required, so a coerced person's emancipation is invalid. The Hanafi school rejects this because the text equates coercion and intoxication in divorce, and by analogy in emancipation.
The practical difference (thamarah): under Hanafi, the slave becomes free immediately; under Shafi'i, freedom is not granted.

Source Text

و عتق المكره والسكران واقع لصدور الركن من الاهل في المحل كمافي الطلاق وقدبيناه من قبل
ترجمہ اور جس شخص کو غلام آزاد کرنے پر مجبور کیا گیا۔ (اس کا ) آزاد کرنا اور نشہ سے مست آدمی کا آزاد کرنا واقع ہے۔ کیونکہ آزاد
The emancipation of a coerced person and a drunkard occurs. And the emancipation of the coerced and the drunkard is valid because the pillar emanates from a qualified person in its place, as in divorce, and we have explained it before. Translation: And a person who was forced to free a slave, his freeing, and the freeing of an intoxicated person, is valid. Because free...
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is a ruling statement affirming that emancipation by a person who is coerced (mukrah) or intoxicated (sakran) is legally valid. The reasoning is analogical to divorce (talaq), where coercion and intoxication do not invalidate the pronouncement. The pillar of emancipation (utterance by a qualified person) is present, so the effect occurs. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that these states do not remove legal capacity for such pronouncements, unlike in some other schools.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): A person who is forced under threat to pronounce emancipation against his will.
سكران (sakran): A person who is intoxicated to the extent of losing reason but still capable of utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The emancipation is valid only if he later ratifies it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives effect to the pronouncement of emancipation even under coercion or intoxication, as long as the person is qualified to utter it.

Freeing a Slave for Allah, Shaytan, or Idol
Ruling that emancipation is valid regardless of intended dedication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that emancipation is valid irrespective of intended dedication and explain why intention does not affect legal validity.

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid by utterance alone; sinful intention does not nullify freedom.

This passage addresses a critical distinction between the essential elements (rukn) of an act and its incidental descriptions. You might assume that a valid emancipation requires a pious intention, but the Hanafi school holds otherwise. Let's explore the reasoning: the rukn is the utterance by a qualified owner; any extra description like 'for Allah' is not a condition. Thus, even dedicating to shaytan or an idol results in freedom, though the free-er is sinful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is emancipation (itq). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) — the slave becomes free — regardless of whether the free-er dedicates it to Allah, shaytan, or an idol.
The ruling is explicit: 'the slave becomes free' in all three cases.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is that the rukn (essential element) of emancipation is present: the qualified person (free, sane, adult owner) and the proper place (his own slave). The utterance of itq fulfills the rukn.
The text states: 'because the rukn of emancipation is present from its qualified person in its proper place'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'for the sake of Allah' is described as an 'addition' (ziyadah), not a condition. Therefore, its absence when dedicating to shaytan or idol does not affect the rukn.
The text: 'the description of nearness (qurbah) in the first phrase is an addition; so its absence in the other two phrases does not impair the emancipation'.
4
Note the ethical consequence
Although the emancipation is valid, the free-er is sinful in the latter two cases because he intends dedication to shaytan or an idol, which is disobedience.
The text clarifies: 'the free-er will be sinful, although the slave becomes free'.
5
Evaluate the principle
Emancipation is an isqat (removal of ownership) and does not require niyyah of qurbah for its validity. The intention affects reward/punishment but not the legal effect.
This distinguishes it from acts that are purely devotional ('ibadat) where intention is a condition for validity.

Source Text

ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے اپنے غلام کو اللہ کی خوشنودی حاصل کرنے کے واسطے ) آزاد کیا یا شیطان کے واسطے یا بت کے واسطے تو غلام (بہر صورت) آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آزاد کر نے کارکن اس کے اہل سے اس کے محل میں پایا گیا اور لفظ اول میں قربت ( لوجہ اللہ ) کا ذکر زیادتی ہے۔ پس بعد والے دونوں لفظوں میں اس کے نہ ہونے سے خلل واقع نہیں ہوگا۔
تشریح مسئلہ ! جس شخص نے اپنے غلام کو اللہ کے واسطے آزاد کیا یا شیطان کے واسطے یا بت کے واسطے آزاد کیا تو غلام بہر صورت آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ دلیل یہ کہ رکن اعتاق یعنی لفظ اعتاق اس کے اہل سے اور اس کے محل میں پایا گیا۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے والا خود آزاد عاقل بالغ اور غلام کا مالک ہے اور غلام اس کا مملوک ہے اور پہلی صورت میں اللہ تعالیٰ کے واسطے کہنا ضروری نہیں بلکہ ایک زائد چیز ہے۔ لہذا شیطان یا بت کی صورت میں اس کا نہ ہونا کچھ مضر نہیں ہے۔ لیکن ان دونوں صورتوں میں آزاد کرنے والا گنہگار ہو گا۔ اگر چہ غلام آزاد ہو جائیگا۔ کیونکہ شیطان اور بت کی صورت میں زیادہ سے زیادہ قرابت الا اذاب کی نفی ہوتی ہے۔ مگر قرابت کی نفی آزادی کی نفی کو مستلزم نہیں ہے۔
And whoever frees a slave for the sake of Allah, or for the shaytan, or for the idol, the slave becomes free, because the rukn (essential element) of emancipation is present from its qualified person in its proper place. And the description of nearness (qurbah) in the first phrase is an addition; so the absence of it in the other two phrases does not impair the emancipation. Translation: And the person who freed his slave for the sake of gaining Allah's pleasure, or for the shaytan, or for the idol, then the slave (in any case) becomes free. Because the rukn of freeing is present from its qualified person and in its proper place, and in the first word, the mention of qurbah (li wajh Allah) is an addition. So its absence in the other two words does not cause any defect. Explanation: The person who freed his slave for Allah, or for shaytan, or for the idol, then the slave becomes free in any case. The proof: The rukn of itq, i.e., the word itq, is present from its qualified person and in its proper place, because the free-er is himself free, sane, adult, and the owner of the slave, and the slave is his owned property. And in the first case, saying 'for Allah' is not necessary; rather it is an extra thing. Therefore, in the case of shaytan or idol, its absence does not harm. However, in these two cases, the free-er will be sinful, although the slave becomes free. Because in the case of shaytan and idol, at most there is a negation of nearness (qurbah). But negation of nearness does not entail negation of freedom.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a person declares emancipation of his slave, even if he dedicates it to shaytan or an idol, the slave becomes free. The reasoning is that the essential element (rukn) of emancipation is the utterance of the word of itq (or any clear expression) by a qualified person (free, adult, sane, owner) regarding his own slave. Any additional description like 'for the sake of Allah' is not a condition for validity; it is merely a description of intention. Thus, even if the intention is sinful (like dedicating to shaytan), the legal effect of freedom takes place. The passage also notes that the person is sinful in the latter two cases, but that does not nullify the freedom. This is based on the principle that acts of worship (qurbah) require proper intention (niyyah) for reward, but emancipation is an isqat (removal of ownership) and does not require niyyah of qurbah for its validity. The authority is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
رکن اعتاق (rukn al-i'taq): The essential element of emancipation: utterance of a clear expression of freeing by the owner (free, sane, adult) regarding his own slave.
قربت (qurbah): Nearness to Allah; an act done to seek reward. Here, it is an additional description, not a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The slave becomes free, and the man is rewarded.

Key Takeaway
The validity of emancipation depends only on the rukn (utterance with capacity), not on the intention of seeking reward.

Emancipation under Duress or Intoxication
Ruling that forced or intoxicated emancipation is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced or intoxicated emancipation and explain the qiyas to divorce.

Core Idea
Coercion and intoxication do not invalidate emancipation because the rukn is present.

This lesson addresses two scenarios: a person forced to free his slave, and an intoxicated person who frees his slave. In both cases, the Hanafi school holds the emancipation valid. The reasoning relies on a qiyas (analogy) to divorce, where the same principle applies. We will unpack the conditions, proof, and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage to determine the act (emancipation by mukrah and sakran) and the hukm (valid).
The text states 'waqi’' (valid).
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The author cites qiyas to talaq (divorce). Identify the asl, far', and illa.
The asl is talaq; the far' is itq; the illa is that both are isqat (removal of a right).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain why the rukn of emancipation suffices: it issues from a qualified person (ahl) in the proper place (mahall).
Qualified means free, adult, owner. Proper place means the slave is owned.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may differ, requiring full volition.
The Hanafi position is based on the nature of isqat actions.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the emancipation is valid and irreversible.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و عتق المكره والسكران واقع لصدور الركن من الاهل في المحل كمافي الطلاق وقدبيناه من قبل
ترجمہ اور جس شخص کو غلام آزاد کرنے پر مجبور کیا گیا۔ (اس کا ) آزاد کرنا اور نشہ سے مست آدمی کا آزاد کرنا واقع ہے۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے کارکن اپنے اہل سے محل میں صادر ہوا ہے۔ جیسے طلاق میں ہے اور ہم اس کو سابق میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص کو اپنا غلام آزاد کرنے پر مجبور کیا گیا۔ پس اس نے آزاد کیا یا نشہ سے مست آدمی نے اپنا غلام آزاد کیا تو وہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے کا رکن بھی اس کے اہل یعنی عاقل بالغ سے صادر ہوا ہے اور منسوب بھی محل کی طرف کیا گیا ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ غلام عبد ملوک ہے۔ جیسا کہ کتاب الطلاق میں یہ مسئلہ بالمفصیل بیان ہو چکا ہے۔
And the emancipation of one who is coerced (mukrah) and one who is intoxicated (sakran) is valid (waqi'), because the rukn of emancipation issues from a qualified person in the proper place, as in the case of talaq (divorce), and we have explained it previously. Translation: And the person who was forced to free his slave (his freeing) and the freeing by a person intoxicated is valid. Because the rukn of freeing issued from its qualified person in the proper place. Just as in talaq, and we have explained this in the previous (discussion). Explanation: If a person was forced to free his slave and he freed, or an intoxicated person freed his slave, then that slave becomes free. Because the rukn of freeing issued from its qualified person, i.e., a sane adult, and it is attributed to the proper place, because the slave is a possessed slave. As this matter has been explained in detail in the Book of Talaq.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses two cases: a person coerced (mukrah) to free his slave, and an intoxicated person (sakran) who frees his slave. In both cases, the emancipation is valid and the slave is freed. The reasoning is that the rukn (essential element) of emancipation is present: the person is a qualified free adult owner (ahl), and the slave is the proper object (mahall). Coercion and intoxication do not negate the capacity for legal actions (ahliyyah al-ada') in the Hanafi school, unlike some other schools. The text draws an analogy to divorce (talaq), where the same ruling applies: coerced and intoxicated divorces are valid. This is based on the principle that these actions are isqat (removal of a right) and do not require full volition. The passage notes that this has been detailed earlier in the Book of Talaq. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
مکره (mukrah): One who is coerced or forced to perform an action under threat.
سکران (sakran): One who is intoxicated (drunk).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The slave is not freed because he was coerced.
B) The slave is freed, and the man is excused.
C) The slave is freed, but the man must pay compensation.
D) The freeing is suspended until the coercion ends.

Key Takeaway
Coerced and intoxicated emancipation is valid in Hanafi law because the rukn is present from a qualified person in the proper place, as established by qiyas to talaq.

Conditional Emancipation: Adding to Ownership or Condition
Ruling on conditional emancipation and its rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that conditional emancipation (ta'liq bi al-milk and ta'liq bi al-shart) is valid, and explain the rationale based on the distinction between isqat and tamlik.

Core Idea
Emancipation is isqat (removal), not tamlik, so it can be conditional — unlike sales or gifts.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principles governing emancipation (itq) are closely tied to those of divorce (talaq). Just as talaq can be made conditional on future events, so too can emancipation. This lesson examines two forms of conditional emancipation: one tied to the acquisition of ownership, and the other tied to a separate condition. The key distinction lies in whether the action is an isqat (removal of ownership) or a tamlik (transfer of ownership).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Forms
Note the two types of conditional emancipation: (1) adding emancipation to ownership (e.g., 'if I own you, you are free'), and (2) suspending it on a condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are free'). These are treated as distinct but both valid in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
For both types, the Hanafi ruling is that the emancipation is valid (sahih) and takes effect when the condition is fulfilled. The text affirms this by comparing to talaq.
3
Recognize the Shafi'i Disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees specifically with the first type (ta'liq bi al-milk). He requires that the speaker own the slave at the time of utterance. Understand that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
The Hanafi response is analogized to talaq and detailed in Kitab al-Talaq.
4
Understand the Rationale: Isqat vs Tamlik
The text explains that itq is fundamentally an isqat (removal of ownership), even if it implicitly affirms power over the slave. Isqat actions can be conditional because they involve giving up a right. In contrast, tamlikat (transfers of ownership like sale or gift) cannot be conditional because they require immediate transfer.
This principle is established in Usul al-Fiqh.
5
Apply to an Example
Consider: A man says to a slave he does not yet own, 'If I buy you, you are free.' According to Hanafi fiqh, when he buys the slave, the emancipation takes effect automatically. In Shafi'i fiqh, the statement is ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان اضاف العتق الى ملک او شرط صح کمافی الطلاق اما الاضافة الى الملك ففيه خلاف الشافعي وقد بیناه في كتاب الطلاق واما التعليق بالشرط فلانه اسقاط فيجرى فيه التعليق بخلاف التمليكات على ماعرف في موضعه
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی شخص نے آزادی کو مالک ہونے کی طرف منسوب کیا تو صحیح ہے۔ جیسے طلاق میں (صحیح) ہے۔ لیکن ملک کی طرف نسبت کرنے میں امام شافعی کا ختلاف ہے اور ہم اسکو کتاب الطلاق میں بیان کر چکے اور شرط پر معلق کرنا اسلئے جائز ہے کہ آزاد کرنا ، اسقاط ہے تو اس میں تعلیق شرط جاری ہو سکتی ہے۔ برخلاف تملیکات کے۔ چنانچہ ( اصول فقہ میں ) اپنے موقع پر معلوم ہو چکا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے آزادی کو ملکیت کی طرف منسوب کیا مثلاً کہا ان ملکتک فانت حر (اگر میں تیرا مالک ہوں تو ، تو آزاد ہے ) یا شرط کی طرف نسبت کی ۔ مثلا کہا ان دخلت الدار فانت حر (اگر تو گھر میں داخل ہوا تو ، تو آزاد ہے ) تو یہ صحیح ہے۔ جیسے طلاق کو ملک یا شرط کی طرف منسوب کرنا صحیح ہے۔ لیکن ملک کی طرف نسبت کرنے میں امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے۔ یعنی ان کے نزدیک آزادی واقع نہیں ہوتی اور ہمارے نزدیک واقع ہو جاتی ہے اور یہ مسئلہ بالتفصیل کتاب الطلاق میں آچکا ہے اور آزادی کو شرط پر معلق کرنا اسلئے جائز ہے کہ آزاد کرنا بالقصد اسقاط ہے۔ اگر چہ ضمنا اثبات قوت ہے اور چونکہ اسقاطات کو شرط پر معلق کرنا جائز ہے۔ اسلئے آزاد کرنے کو بھی معلق کرنا درست ہو گا۔ اس کے برخلاف تملیکات یعنی جن صورتوں میں مالک کرنے کے معنی پائے جاتے ہیں ان کو شرط پر معلق کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ اصول فقہ میں اپنے موقع پر معلوم ہو چکا ہے۔
And if he adds the emancipation to ownership or a condition, it is valid, as in the case of talaq. As for adding it to ownership, there is a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, and we have explained it in the Book of Talaq. As for suspending it on a condition, it is because it (emancipation) is an isqat (removal), so suspension (ta'liq) is applicable, unlike tamlikat (transfers of ownership), as has been established in its place. Translation: And if a person attributed freedom to being an owner (i.e., said 'if I am your owner, you are free') or to a condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are free'), then it is valid. Just as attributing talaq to ownership or condition is valid. But regarding attribution to ownership, Imam al-Shafi'i differs, and we have explained this in the Book of Talaq. And suspending on a condition is permissible because freeing is an isqat, so ta'liq can apply to it, unlike tamlikat, as has been established in its place in Usul al-Fiqh. Explanation: If a person attributed freedom to ownership, e.g., 'If I am your owner, you are free,' or to a condition, e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are free,' this is valid. Just as attributing talaq to ownership or condition is valid. But about attribution to ownership, Imam al-Shafi'i differs; in his view, freedom does not occur, while in our view it does. And this has been detailed in the Book of Talaq. And suspending freedom on a condition is permissible because freeing is fundamentally an isqat (removal), even though it implicitly includes affirmation (ithbat) of power. Since isqatat can be conditional, ta'liq in itq is valid. In contrast, tamlikat (transactions transferring ownership) cannot be conditional. This is established in Usul al-Fiqh.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage discusses two ways of making emancipation conditional: (1) adding it to ownership (ta'liq bi al-milk), e.g., saying 'if I own you, you are free' (which would apply when someone buys a slave), and (2) suspending it on a condition (ta'liq bi al-shart), e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are free.' Both are valid in the Hanafi school. The text notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees with the first type (ta'liq bi al-milk), arguing that emancipation cannot be retroactive or conditional on ownership because ownership must be present at the time of utterance. However, the Hanafi view, elaborated in the Book of Talaq, is that it is valid. For conditional emancipation (ta'liq bi al-shart), the rationale is that itq is an isqat (removal of ownership), and isqatat can be suspended on conditions, unlike tamlikat (transactions that create ownership, like sale or gift), which cannot be conditional because they involve transferring property and require immediate delivery. This distinction is based on usul al-fiqh principles. The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
تعلیق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending the effect of a legal action on the occurrence of a future condition.
اسقاط (isqat): Removal of a right or ownership; contrasted with tamlik (transfer of ownership).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because itq is a form of tamlik.
B) Because itq is an isqat, and isqat can be conditional.
C) Because itq is a wajib act.
D) Because itq is a contract like sale.

Key Takeaway
Both forms of conditional emancipation are valid in Hanafi fiqh because itq is an act of isqat, not tamlik, and isqat can be suspended on conditions.

Harbi's Slave Becoming Muslim in Dar al-Islam
Ruling with proof: automatic freedom of a harbi's slave upon conversion and entry into Dar al-Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam, and explain the dual proof (hadith and principle) that supports it.

Core Idea
A harbi's slave who enters Dar al-Islam as a Muslim becomes free automatically — no Muslim can be enslaved.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: what happens when a slave belonging to a non-Muslim enemy (harbi) embraces Islam and flees to Muslim territory. The Hanafi ruling is clear: the slave is free. The text provides two proofs: a prophetic hadith regarding the slaves of Ta'if, and a general legal principle that a Muslim cannot be subjected to slavery ab initio. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Islamic law prioritizes the status of a Muslim over property rights of a non-Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon: a harbi's slave becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam. The ruling is that he becomes free.
The act combines conversion and physical entry into Muslim territory.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: the hadith about the slaves of Ta'if ('They are the freed ones of Allah') and the general principle that no Muslim can be enslaved initially.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so the ruling is wajib not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's declaration directly indicates freedom. The principle prevents re-enslavement of a Muslim. Together they establish automatic freedom upon entry.
The principle 'la istirqaq 'ala al-muslim ibtida'an' is a key Hanafi maxim.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars argue the slave remains property and must be ransomed. The Hanafi tarjih gives automatic freedom based on the explicit hadith.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi view, the slave is immediately free with no payment required; opposing view would require ransom or return.
This affects the obligations of the Muslim state and the slave's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ اور جب کسی حربی کا فر کا غلام مسلمان ہو کر ہمارے یہاں ( دارالاسلام میں ) چلا آیا تو وہ آزاد ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ حضور اقدس صلى الله عليه وسلم نے طائف کے غلاموں کو جب مسلمان ہو کر آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم کے حضور میں چلے آئے تھے۔ فرمایا کہ یہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے آزاد کئے ہوئے ہیں اور اسلئے کہ اس غلام نے اپنی جان کو ایسی حالت میں محفوظ کر لیا کہ وہ مسلمان ہے اور ابتداء کسی مسلمان پر غلامی نہیں ہوسکتی ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ! اگر حربی کافر کا غلام مسلمان ہو کر دار الاسلام میں آگیا تو وہ آزاد ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جس وقت طائف کے غلام جن کی
And when a slave of a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) comes out to us as a Muslim, he becomes free, because of his (peace be upon him) statement regarding the slaves of Ta'if when they came out to him as Muslims: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And because he secured his person while being Muslim, and there can be no initial enslavement of a Muslim. Translation: And when a slave of a harbi infidel comes to us (Dar al-Islam) as a Muslim, he becomes free. Because the Holy Prophet (peace be upon him) said about the slaves of Ta'if when they came out to him as Muslims: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And because this slave secured his life in a state of being Muslim, and initially a Muslim cannot be enslaved. Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that if a slave belonging to a harbi (a non-Muslim from enemy territory) becomes a Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam (Muslim territory), he is automatically freed. The evidence is twofold: first, a hadith about the slaves of Ta'if who came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) as Muslims, and he declared them 'freed ones of Allah.' Second, a general principle: a Muslim cannot be enslaved ab initio (initially); slavery is only permissible for non-Muslims captured in war. Since the slave has embraced Islam and placed himself under Muslim protection, he cannot be subjected to slavery. The ruling is based on the idea that the slave has secured his person (ahraza nafsahu) in a state of Islam. This is a specific application of the broader rule that no Muslim can be enslaved. The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah, though the text is cut off at the page end.

Key Terms
حربی (harbi): A non-Muslim from enemy territory (dar al-harb) who is not under Muslim protection.
استرقاق (istirqaq): The act of enslaving someone. The principle is that a Muslim cannot be enslaved initially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

6 min
A Harbi's Slave Who Becomes Muslim and Enters Dar al-Islam is Free
Ruling with proof on automatic freedom of Harbi's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding a Harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam, and identify the two proofs (hadith and legal principle) that support it.

Core Idea
A Harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam becomes free automatically — the Prophet declared such slaves 'freed ones of Allah'.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a fiqh ruling concerning the status of a slave belonging to a Harbi (enemy non-Muslim) who embraces Islam and enters the land of Islam. The ruling is that such a slave becomes free immediately upon entry, based on a hadith of the Prophet (SAW) and the fundamental principle that a Muslim cannot be initially enslaved. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions: the slave must be from a Harbi (not a Dhimmi), must actively become Muslim, and must physically enter Dar al-Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? A slave owned by a Harbi becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam. The ruling is that he becomes free.
The act is the slave's entry; the consequence is freedom.
2
Note the conditions
The ruling applies only when three conditions are met: (1) the original owner is a Harbi, (2) the slave becomes Muslim, (3) the slave physically enters Dar al-Islam. If any condition is missing, the slave does not become free.
A slave of a Dhimmi who converts does not become free.
3
Locate the first proof: hadith
The text cites the hadith of the slaves of Ta'if: when they came to the Prophet (SAW) as Muslims, he said 'They are the freed ones of Allah' (hum 'utaqa' Allah). This indicates that any such slave who comes to Dar al-Islam as a Muslim is free.
The hadith is a clear textual proof.
4
Locate the second proof: legal principle
The second proof is rational: the slave secured himself (ahraza nafsahu) while being a Muslim, and a Muslim cannot be enslaved initially (la istirqaq 'ala al-muslim ibtida'an). Therefore, by entering Dar al-Islam as a Muslim, the slave's freedom is established.
This principle is a foundational axiom of Islamic law.
5
Understand the scope
This ruling is specific to Harbi slaves. A Dhimmi's slave does not become free by converting because he is already under Islamic jurisdiction and the principle of initial enslavement does not apply to him in the same way.
The distinction between Harbi and Dhimmi is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حربی کا غلام دار الاسلام مسلمان ہو کر آ گیا آزاد ہو جائے گا
و اذا خرج عبد الحربي الينا مسلما عتق لقوله عليه السلام فى عبيد الطائف حين خرجوا اليه مسلمین هم عتقاء الله ولانه احرز نفسه و هو مسلم ولا استرقاق على المسلم ابتداءً
ترجمہ اور جب کسی حربی کا فر کا غلام مسلمان ہو کر ہمارے یہاں ( دارالاسلام میں ) چلا آیا تو وہ آزاد ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ حضور اقدس صلى الله عليه وسلم نے طائف کے غلاموں کو جب مسلمان ہو کر آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم کے حضور میں چلے آئے تھے۔ فرمایا کہ یہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے آزاد کئے ہوئے ہیں اور اسلئے کہ اس غلام نے اپنی جان کو ایسی حالت میں محفوظ کر لیا کہ وہ مسلمان ہے اور ابتداء کسی مسلمان پر غلامی نہیں ہوسکتی ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ! اگر حربی کافر کا غلام مسلمان ہو کر دار الاسلام میں آگیا تو وہ آزاد ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جس وقت طائف کے غلام جن کی تعداد ۲۳ تھی۔ مسلمان ہو کر حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کی خدمت میں حاضر ہوئے تو آقاصلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہم عتقاء اللہ یعنی یہ اللہ تعالی کے آزاد کیئے ہوئے ہیں اور دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس غلام نے بحالت اسلام اپنے آپ کو دارالاسلام میں محفوظ کیا ہے اور ابتداء کسی مسلمان کو غلام نہیں بنایا جا سکتا ہے۔ اسلئے یہ آزاد ہوگا۔
The slave of a Harbi, when he enters Dar al-Islam as a Muslim, becomes free. And if the slave of a Harbi comes out to us as a Muslim, he becomes free due to his statement (peace be upon him) concerning the slaves of Ta'if when they came out to him as Muslims: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And because he secured himself while being Muslim, and there is no enslavement upon a Muslim initially. Translation: And when a slave of a Harbi enters our land (Dar al-Islam) as a Muslim, he becomes free. Because the Prophet (SAW) said about the slaves of Ta'if when they came to him as Muslims: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And because this slave secured himself in that state that he is Muslim, and initially no Muslim can be enslaved. Explanation: If a slave of a Harbi becomes Muslim and comes to Dar al-Islam, he will be free. The evidence is that when the slaves of Ta'if, who were 23 in number, became Muslim and came to the Prophet, he said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And the second evidence is that this slave secured himself in Dar al-Islam while being Muslim, and initially a Muslim cannot be enslaved. Therefore, he becomes free.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage gives the Hanafi ruling that a slave belonging to a Harbi (enemy non-Muslim) who embraces Islam and enters Dar al-Islam becomes free automatically. The proof is a hadith: the Prophet (SAW) said about the slaves of Ta'if who came to him as Muslims, 'They are the freed ones of Allah' (hum 'utaqa' Allah). The second proof is a rational and principled one: a Muslim cannot be enslaved ab initio (la istirqaq 'ala al-muslim ibtida'an). Therefore, by entering Islamic territory as a Muslim, the slave's freedom is established. This ruling is specific to slaves of Harbis; slaves of Dhimmis are not included because they already reside in Dar al-Islam.

Key Terms
حربي (ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with the Islamic state; his property, including slaves, are considered enemy property unless they enter Dar al-Islam.
دار الإسلام (dār al-islām): Territory under Islamic rule where Islamic law is applied.
إسترقاق (istirqāq): Enslavement; the principle that a Muslim cannot be enslaved initially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary hadith evidence for the Hanafi ruling on a Harbi's slave who becomes Muslim?
A) The hadith 'Whoever frees a slave, Allah will free him from the fire'
B) The hadith about the slaves of Ta'if: 'They are the freed ones of Allah'
C) The hadith 'There is no compulsion in religion'
D) The hadith 'The best of you are those who feed the slave'

Key Takeaway
A Harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and enters Dar al-Islam is free due to the hadith of Ta'if and the principle that a Muslim cannot be initially enslaved.
