Partial Manumission by an Insolvent Co-Owner: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining Hanafi ruling when one co-owner frees a slave while insolvent, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the silent partner's options when a co-owner frees his share while insolvent, and explain the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Insolvency of the manumitter shifts the burden to the slave, not the silent partner.

When one co-owner frees his share of a jointly-owned slave but is insolvent, the Hanafi school gives the silent partner (sharīk sākit) a choice: either free his own share or make the slave work (sai') to earn the freed share's value. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing the slave cannot be forced to work without consent. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind both positions and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two options the Hanafi school grants the silent partner when his co-owner is insolvent: free his share or make the slave earn.
The silent partner receives wala' for his share whether he frees or makes the slave work.
2
Identify the opponent's view
State the Shafi'i position: the silent partner retains full ownership and can sell or gift his share, but cannot compel the slave to work.
Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with the Sahibayn only when the manumitter is wealthy.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the Shafi'i dalil: the slave neither consented nor committed wrongdoing, so forcing him to work is unjust; also, the silent partner cannot demand compensation from the insolvent manumitter.
The Shafi'i position prioritizes the slave's autonomy and the partner's property rights.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: making the slave work does not require his consent or wrongdoing; it is justified because the silent partner's value is tied up in the slave's person.
The slave's partial freedom and partial slavery can coexist—'strength and weakness cannot combine' but here they do due to the mixed status.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafi – the slave works to earn his freedom and cannot recover from the manumitter; Shafi'i – the silent partner can freely dispose of his share.
The difference affects the slave's path to full freedom and the partner's rights.

غلام کے جو رہن ہو۔ جبکہ اس کو تنگدست راہن نے آزاد کر دیا۔ اسلئے کہ غلام اپنی گردن کیلئے کمائی کرتا ہے۔ حالانکہ (اس کی گردن ) چھوٹ گئی تھی۔ یا اس نے ایسا قرضہ ادا کیا جو راہن پر تھا۔ لہذارا بہن سے واپس لے گا اور امام شافعی کا قول مالدار کے بارے میں صاحبین کے قول کے مانند ہے اور تنگدست کی صورت میں امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ ساکت کا حصہ اس کی ملک پر باقی رہے گا۔ اس کو بیچا بھی جاسکتا ہے اور ہبہ بھی کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے والے کی تنگدستی کی وجہ سے شریک کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ اس سے ضمان لے اور نہ غلام سے کمائی کرانے کا اختیار ہے کیونکہ غلام نہ ظلم کرنے والا ہے اور نہ آزادی پر راضی ہے اور پورے غلام کو آزاد کرنے کی بھی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ (اس میں ) ساکت کا ضر ر ہے۔ پس جو کچھ ہم نے متعین کیا وہ متعین ہو گیا۔ ہم اس کا یہ جواب دیتے ہیں کہ (شریک کی ) مالیت (اس غلام کے پاس رکی ہوئی ہے۔ پس ایک ہی غلام میں دونوں باتیں یعنی قوت جو مالکیت کا سبب ہے اور ضعف جو اس قوت کو سلب کرنے والا ہے، جمع نہ ہوں گی۔ تشریح ماقبل کی تمام تفصیل اس وقت تھی جبکہ آزاد کرنے والا خوشحال ہو اور اگر آزاد کرنے والا مفلس ہو تو شریک ساکت کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ چاہے تو اپنا حصہ آزاد کر دے۔ کیونکہ اس کی ملکیت باقی ہے اور چاہے غلام سے کمائی کرائے ۔ کیونکہ اس کے حصہ کی مالیت غلام کے پاس رکی ہوئی ہے اور جس صورت میں شریک ایسا قرضہ اداء نہیں کرتا جو آزاد کرنے والے پر ہو۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے والے کی مفلسی کی وجہ سے اس پر کچھ واجب نہیں ہے۔ برخلاف اس غلام کے جو رہن ہو اور راہن نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا۔ حالانکہ راہن مرتہن کا قرضہ تنگدستی کی وجہ سے ادا نہیں کر سکتا ہے۔ تو غلام اپنی قیمت کما کر مرتہن کو دے گا اور پھر راہن سے واپس لے گا اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام نے اپنی قیمت کے واسطے کمائی کی ہے۔ حالانکہ اسکی گردن چھوٹ گئی تھی ۔ یا یوں کہہ لیجئے کہ اس نے ایسا قرضہ ادا کیا ہے جو راہن پر تھا۔ اسلئے یہ غلام راہن سے واپس لے گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر آزاد کرنے والا خوشحال ہے تو اس میں امام شافعی کا قول صاحبین کے قول کے مانند ہے۔ لیکن آزاد کرنے والا مفلس ہو تو امام شافعی کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ شریک ساکت کا حصہ اس کی ملک پر باقی رہے گا۔ خواہ وہ اپنا حصہ فروخت کرے خواہ ہیہ کرے۔ یعنی اس کے حصہ میں اسکی پوری ملکیت باقی رہے گی۔ کیونکہ شریک ساکت نہ تو آزاد کرنے والے کو اپنے حصہ کا ضامن بنا سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے والا تنگدست اور مفلس ہے اور نہ غلام سے کمائی کرانے کا اختیار کیونکہ غلام نے کوئی ظلم نہیں کیا اور نہ وہ آزاد کیئے جانے پر راضی ہوا۔ کیونکہ رضا مندی بغیر علم کے مستحق نہیں ہوتی اور یہاں مولیٰ نے بغیر غلام کے علم کے اس کو آزاد کیا ہے۔ لہذا غلام کی رضامندی بھی نہیں پائی گئی اور اس کی بھی کوئی وجہ نہیں کہ پورے غلام کو آزاد قرار دیا جائے۔ کیونکہ اس میں شریک ساکت کا ضرر ہے۔ لہذا جو ہم نے بیان کیا ہے وہی متعین ہے۔ یعنی شریک ساکت اپنے حصہ کا مالک ہے۔ چنانچہ وہ اس کو بیچ بھی سکتا ہے اور ہبہ بھی کر سکتا ہے۔ لیکن ہماری طرف سے اس کا جواب یہ ہو گا کہ شریک ساکت کو غلام سے کمائی کرانے کی راہ موجود ہے۔ کیونکہ کمائی کرانے کیلئے اس کی ضرورت نہیں کہ غلام نے کوئی ظلم اور جرم کیا ہو ۔ بلکہ وہ تو اس بناء پر ہے کہ شریک ساکت کی مالیت اس غلام کے پاس رکی ہوئی ہے تو جب کمائی کرانا ممکن ہے تو ایک ہی غلام میں دونوں باتیں جمع نہ ہوں گی۔ یعنی غلام میں آزاد ہونے کی وجہ سے مالک ہونے کی قوت ہے اور باقی حصہ آزاد نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ قوت نہیں ہے۔
فائده. ولاء، واو کے فتحہ کے ساتھ ، وہ میراث جو آزاد کردہ نظام سے یا عقد موالات کی وجہ سے حاصل ہو۔ (مصباح اللغات)
Book of Manumission. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah, Volume 5. Translation: And in the case of the manumitter's insolvency, if he wishes, he may free (his share). He has the option; and if he wishes, he may make (the slave) earn income, due to the proof we have mentioned. In both cases, the silent partner will receive the wala' (clientage) (for his share), because the manumission is from him. And the one from whom earnings are taken (the slave) cannot take back the money paid from his manumitter. Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree on this. Because the slave works to free his own neck and does not repay a debt that is upon the manumitter, because nothing is obligatory upon the manumitter due to his poverty. Unlike the case of a mortgaged slave when the insolvent mortgagor frees him. Because the slave works for his own neck, even though his neck was already released, or he repays a debt that was upon the mortgagor. Therefore, he will take (the money) back from the mortgagor. And the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the wealthy man resembles the statement of the Sahibayn. In the case of the insolvent, Imam al-Shafi'i said that the silent partner's share remains in his ownership; it can be sold and gifted. Because due to the manumitter's insolvency, the partner does not have the option to take a guarantee from him, nor does he have the option to make the slave earn, because the slave is neither an oppressor nor has he consented to freedom, and there is no reason to declare the entire slave free because that would harm the silent partner. Thus, what we have determined is determined. We respond to this that the (partner's) value is (tied up in the slave). Therefore, two matters—the strength that causes ownership and the weakness that removes that strength—cannot be combined in one slave. Elaboration: All the previous detail was when the manumitter was prosperous. But if the manumitter is insolvent, then the silent partner has the option: if he wishes, he may free his own share, because his ownership remains; and if he wishes, he may make the slave earn income, because the value of his share is tied up in the slave. In the case where the silent partner has made the slave earn, the slave cannot take that money from his manumitter. In this ruling, Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn are both agreed. Because the slave works to free his own neck and does not repay a debt that is upon the manumitter, because due to the manumitter's insolvency, nothing is obligatory upon him. Unlike the slave who is mortgaged and the mortgagor frees him while the mortgagor cannot repay the mortgagee's debt due to poverty. Then the slave will earn his price and give it to the mortgagee, and then take it back from the mortgagor. The proof is that the slave earned for his price even though his neck was released, or you may say that he repaid a debt that was upon the mortgagor. Therefore, this slave will take back from the mortgagor. The author of al-Hidayah says: If the manumitter is prosperous, then the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i is like the statement of the Sahibayn. But if the manumitter is insolvent, then Imam al-Shafi'i's position is that the silent partner's share remains in his ownership, whether he sells his share or gifts it. That is, he has full ownership over his share. Because the silent partner can neither make the manumitter a guarantor for his share (since the manumitter is insolvent), nor does he have the option to make the slave earn, because the slave did not commit any wrong nor did he consent to being freed. Because consent is not valid without knowledge, and here the master freed him without the slave's knowledge. Hence, the slave's consent was not obtained, and there is no reason to declare the entire slave free because that would harm the silent partner. Therefore, what we have stated is determined: the silent partner is the owner of his share. Hence, he can sell it or gift it. But our response is that the silent partner has the way of making the slave earn income. Because to make him earn, it is not necessary that the slave has committed oppression or crime; rather, it is based on the fact that the silent partner's value is tied up in the slave. So when making him earn is possible, then two matters cannot be combined in one slave: that is, the slave has the strength of ownership due to being freed in part, and the weakness of not being freed in the remaining share, so this strength is absent. Benefit: Wala' (with fatha on waw) is the inheritance obtained from a freed slave or through a contract of clientage (muwalat). (Mishbah al-Lughat).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a case where one co-owner (sharik) frees his share of a jointly-owned slave while insolvent (muflis). The Hanafi school, represented by Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), agrees that the silent partner (sharik sākit) has two options: either free his own share as well, or make the slave work (sai') to earn the value of the freed share. The slave cannot later recover that money from the manumitter because the manumitter's insolvency means no obligation lies on him to compensate. The text compares this to a mortgaged slave case where the mortgagor frees the slave while insolvent; there, the slave must work to pay the mortgagee and then recover from the mortgagor, since the slave's neck was already free. Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with the Sahibayn when the manumitter is wealthy, but differs in the insolvent case: he holds that the silent partner retains full ownership over his share, may sell or gift it, and cannot compel the slave to work. His reasoning is that the slave neither committed wrongdoing nor consented to freedom, so he cannot be forced to earn. The Hanafi commentary responds by noting that making the slave earn does not require his wrongdoing or consent; it is justified because the silent partner's property value is tied up in the slave. Hence, the slave's partial freedom and partial servitude can coexist. The passage ends with a lexical benefit (fa'idah) defining wala' as the inheritance relationship from a freed slave or a contract of clientage.

Key Terms
سعاية (sai'ah): The slave working to earn money to pay for his freedom or compensate the owner
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance and clientage that accrues to the manumitter from the freed slave
شریک ساکت (sharīk sākit): The co-owner who does not initiate the manumission but remains silent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what options does the silent partner (sharīk sākit) have when his co-owner frees his share of a slave while insolvent?
A) Only free his own share
B) Only make the slave work (sai')
C) Either free his own share or make the slave work
D) Sell or gift his share as he pleases

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the silent partner a choice to either free his share or make the slave work, with the slave unable to recover from the insolvent manumitter.

Two Partners Each Accusing the Other of Manumission
Explaining the ruling when two co-owners each testify that the other freed the slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the slave's work (sai'ah) when co-owners accuse each other of manumission, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) based on solvency.

Core Idea
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, whether the slave works depends on the solvency of the co-owners and the possibility of claiming indemnity.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines a scenario where two co-owners of a slave each testify that the other freed the slave. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, offer different rulings on whether the slave must work (sai'ah) to compensate his owners. The key factor is whether the accusing partner can claim indemnity from the other, which in turn depends on solvency. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of istihsan and tadmin within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and dispute
Identify that two co-owners each testify the other freed the shared slave. Know that this creates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Outline Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave must work for both partners regardless of solvency. Understand his reasoning: certainty of right to make the slave work, and impossibility of indemnity due to denial.
3
Outline the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differentiate based on solvency: if both wealthy, no work; if both insolvent, work for both; if one wealthy and one insolvent, work only for the wealthy.
Note the three-case division.
4
Analyse the reasoning
Abu Hanifa emphasizes the certain right to work, while the Sahibayn allow substitution by indemnity (tadmin) when the other partner is solvent.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The slave's obligation to work and the distribution of wala' differ. According to Abu Hanifa, wala' goes to each for his share; according to Sahibayn, wala' is suspended until resolved.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو شهد كل واحد من الشريكين على صاحبه بالعتق سعى العبد لكل واحد منهما في نصيبه موسرين كانا او معسرين عند ابي حنيفة وكذا اذا كان احدهما موسرا و الآخر معسر الان كل واحد منهما يزعم ان صاحبه اعتق نصيبه فصار مكاتبافي زعمه عنده وحرم عليه الاسترقاق فيصدق في حق نفسه فيمنع من استرقاقه ويستسعيه لاناتيقنا بحق الاستسعاء كاذبا كان او صادقا لانه مكاتبه او مملوكه فلهذا يستسعيانه و لا يختلف ذالك باليسار و الاعسار لان حقه في الحالين فى احدشيأين لان يسار المعتق لا يمنع السعاية عنده وقد تعذر التضمين لانكار الشريك فتعين الأخر وهو السعاية والولاء لهما لان كلامنهما يقول عتق نصیب صاحبی علیه باعتاقة وولا وه له وعتق نصيبى بالسعاية وولاؤه لى وقال ابو يوسف و محمد ان كانا موسرين فلا سعاية عليه لان كل واحد منهما يبرأ عن سعايته بدعوى الضمان على صاحبه لان يسار المعتق يمنع السعاية عندهما الا ان الدعوى لم تثبت لانكارا لأخرو البراءة عن السعاية قد تثبت لاقراره على نفسه وان كانا معسرين سعى لهمالان كل واحد منهما يدعى السعاية عليه صادقا كان او كاذبا على ما بيناه اذ المعتق معسر و أن كان احدهما موسر او الأخر معسر اسعى للموسر منهما لانه لا يدعى الضمان على صاحبه لاعساره و انما يدعى عليه السعاية ولا يتبر أعنه ولا يسعى للمعسر منهما لانه يدعى الضمان على صاحبه ليساره فيكون مبريا للعبد عن السعاية والولاء موقوف فى جميع ذالك عند همالان كل واحد منهما يحد. صاحبه وهو يتبرأ عنه فيبقى موقوفا الى ان يتفقا على اعتاق احدهم
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا کہ اگر دونوں شریکوں میں سے ہر ایک نے اپنے ساتھی پر ( غلام ) آزاد کرنے کی گواہی دی نہ تو غلام ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کیلئے اس کے حصہ میں کمائی کرے گا۔ خواہ دونوں خوشحال ہوں یا مفلس ہوں ۔ ( یہ ) ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور
He (al-Quduri) said: And if each of the two partners testifies against his partner that he freed (the slave), then the slave will work (sai') for each of them in his share, whether they are wealthy or insolvent, according to Abu Hanifa. Likewise, if one is wealthy and the other insolvent. Because each of them claims that his partner freed his share, so in his claim he becomes a mukatab (contract-slave) in his (Abu Hanifa's) view, and it becomes prohibited to enslave him. So he is believed regarding himself and is prevented from being enslaved, and he (the claiming partner) makes him work (yustas'ihi) because we are certain of the right to make him work, whether he is lying or truthful, because he is either his mukatab or his slave. Therefore, they both make him work, and this does not differ with wealth or insolvency, because his right in both cases is one of two things. Because the solvency of the manumitter does not prevent the work (sai'ah) in his view, and the claim for indemnity (tadmin) is impossible due to the partner's denial, so the other option is determined, which is the work (sai'ah). And the wala' (clientage) belongs to both of them, because each of them says: 'My partner's share was freed by his manumission and his wala' goes to him, and my share is freed by the work (sai'ah) and its wala' goes to me.' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If both are wealthy, then no work is required from the slave, because each of them is absolved from making him work by claiming indemnity (tadmin) from his partner, because the solvency of the manumitter prevents the work in their view. However, the claim is not established due to the other's denial, but absolution from work can be established due to his admission against himself. If both are insolvent, then the slave works for both, because each of them claims the work against him, whether he is truthful or lying, as we have explained, since the manumitter is insolvent. If one is wealthy and the other insolvent, then the slave works for the wealthy one because he does not claim indemnity against his partner due to the partner's insolvency, but only claims the work, and he is not absolved from it. And he does not work for the insolvent one, because he claims indemnity against his partner due to his solvency, thus absolving the slave from work. And the wala' is suspended in all these cases according to them, because each of them is accusing his partner, and he is absolving him, so it remains suspended until they agree on the manumission of one of them. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If each of the two partners testifies against his partner that he freed the slave, then the slave will work for each of them in his share, whether both are wealthy or insolvent. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a scenario where two co-owners of a slave each testify that the other one freed the slave. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, regardless of whether both are wealthy or insolvent, the slave must work (sai'ah) for each partner to earn the value of their respective shares. Each partner claims that the other manumitted his share, so in each claim, the slave is considered a mukatab (contract-slave) relative to that partner. The slave is believed regarding his own status, and it is forbidden to enslave him. The partner who claims the manumission has the right to make him work, because he is either his mukatab or his slave. Wala' (clientage) follows accordingly: each partner gets the wala' of his own share. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differ: if both partners are wealthy, the slave does not work because each can claim indemnity (tadmin) from the other solvent partner, and in their view, the solvency of the manumitter prevents the work. If both are insolvent, the slave works for both. If one is wealthy and the other insolvent, the slave works only for the wealthy partner because he cannot claim indemnity from the insolvent one, but he can claim work; the insolvent partner is deemed to have absolved the slave by claiming indemnity. The wala' is suspended in the Sahibayn's view until the dispute is resolved. The passage cuts off mid-sentence but is a complete legal unit of the dispute's core.

Key Terms
سعاية (sai'ah): The slave working to earn money to pay for his freedom or compensate the owner
ضمين (tadmin): Claiming indemnity or compensation from the other partner for the value of the share
ولاء (wala'): Clientage relationship that transfers inheritance rights to the manumitter

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when each co-owner testifies that the other freed the slave, what is the status of the slave?
A) The slave is completely free
B) The slave must work for both partners
C) The slave works only for the wealthier partner
D) The slave is owned by the partner who first testified

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when co-owners accuse each other of manumission, the slave must work unless the accusing partner can claim indemnity from a solvent co-owner.

Slave's Earnings in Partner Dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave's obligation to earn for both partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the obligation of a slave to earn (saiyah) when a co-owner claims the other freed his share, and apply the correct ruling based on the partners' financial status.

Core Idea
A partner's claim binds himself, but not the other; earnings depend on the partner's wealth according to Sahibayn.

When a co-owner of a slave claims that the other co-owner freed his share, a legal problem arises: should the slave be required to earn (saiyah) to compensate the claimant? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagree on this issue. In this lesson, we will unpack their respective positions, the reasoning behind them, and the practical consequences. Although this scenario is less common today, it illustrates important Hanafi principles about property rights, claims, and the relationship between slavery and manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Two partners jointly own a slave. One partner (claimant) says to the other: 'You freed your share of the slave.' The other partner denies this. What are the rights and obligations of the slave, the claimant, and the denying partner?
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave must earn for both partners the value of each share, regardless of whether the partners are wealthy or poor. The wala (clientage) belongs to both partners.
His rationale: each partner's claim is accepted with respect to his own rights, making the slave a mukatab for that partner. Since the other partner denies manumission, compensation is impossible, so the only remedy is the slave's earnings.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn differentiate based on the financial status of the partners. If both are wealthy, the slave does not earn; if both are poor, the slave earns for both; if one is wealthy and the other poor, the slave earns only for the wealthy partner. The wala is suspended until the partners agree on who actually manumitted.
Their rationale: a wealthy manumitter is able to pay compensation, so the claimant claims compensation from him, not earnings from the slave. A poor manumitter cannot compensate, so the claimant can demand earnings.
4
Identify the Practical Difference (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave always works for both partners. Under the Sahibayn, the outcome varies: if both wealthy, the slave is free from earning; if both poor, he earns for both; if mixed, he earns only for the wealthy partner. Also, wala belongs to both under Abu Hanifa, but is suspended under the Sahibayn.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preference) if Applicable
The passage does not indicate which view is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa). Both views are recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah. In later Hanafi practice, the view of the Sahibayn often prevails in matters involving compensation, but here no explicit tarjih is given.

مالدار اور دوسرا مفلس ہو۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ دونوں شریکوں میں سے ہر ایک کا خیال یہ ہے کہ اس کے دوسرے شریک نے اپنا حصہ آزاد کیا ہے۔ لہذا اسکے خیال کے مطابق یہ غلام مکاتب ہو گیا۔ جیسا کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کا قول ہے۔ اب اس پر اس غلام کو رقیق بنانا حرام ہو گیا۔ پس شریک کا گمان اگر چہ دوسرے شریک کے حق میں صحیح نہ ہو لیکن خود اسکے حق میں تصدیق کی جائیگی۔ اس وجہ سے اس کو منع کیا جائے گا کہ وہ اس غلام کو رقیق یا تنگدستی کی وجہ سے مختلف نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کا حق جس نے دوسرے شریک پر اپنے حصہ کو آزاد کرنے کا دعویٰ کیا ہے۔ دونوں صورتوں میں دو باتوں میں سے ایک ہے۔ یعنی خوشحالی کی صورت میں تاوان لینا یا کمائی کرانا۔ کیونکہ آزاد کرنے والے کی خوشحالی امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس بات سے نہیں روکتی کہ غلام سے کمائی کرائے اور یہاں تاوان لینا ناممکن ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ شریک آزاد کرنے سے منکر ہے بلکہ الٹا مدعی ہے کہ اس کے شریک نے غلام آزاد کیا ہے۔ پس جب تاوان لینا نا ممکن ہو گیا تو دوسری بات رہ گئی یعنی یہی اختیار رہا کہ غلام سے کمائی کرائے اور اس غلام کی ولاء دونوں شریکوں کی ہوگی کیونکہ دونوں میں سے ہر شریک کہتا ہے کہ دوسرے کا حصہ اس کے آزاد کرنے سے آزاد ہوا۔ جس کی ولاء اسی کی ہے اور میرا حصہ کمائی کر دینے سے آزاد ہوا اور اس مسئلہ میں صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اگر دونوں شریک خوشحال ہوں تو غلام پر کمائی واجب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ ہر شریک اس غلام کو کمائی سے بری کرتا ہے اسلئے کہ اپنے شریک پر تاوان کا دعوی کرتا ہے۔ کیونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک جب آزاد کرنے والا خوشحال ہو تو غلام پر سعایت لازم نہیں ہوتی۔ مگر اس کا دعوی اپنے شریک پر اس وجہ سے ثابت نہ ہوا کہ وہ انکار کرتا ہے اور غلام کا کمائی سے بری ہونا ثابت ہو گیا کیونکہ اس کا اقرار اپنی ذات پر لازم ہے۔ اور اگر دونوں شریک مفلس ہوں تو غلام دونوں کے واسطے کمائی کرے کیونکہ ہر ایک شریک اس غلام پر کمائی کا مدعی ہے۔ خواہ وہ سچا ہو یا جھوٹا ہو۔ کیونکہ غلام پر کمائی واجب ہونا یقینی ہے اسلئے کہ آزاد کرنے والا مفلس ہے اور اگر دونوں میں سے ایک خوشحال ہو اور دوسرا مفلس ہو تو غلام خوشحال کے واسطے کمائی کرے کیونکہ وہ اپنے شریک پر اس کی تنگدستی کی وجہ سے ضمان کا دعوی نہیں کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ غلام پر کمائی کا دعوی کرتا ہے۔ لہذا غلام کمائی کرنے سے بری نہ ہو گا اور مفلس شریک کیلئے غلام کمائی نہ کرے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنے شریک پر تاوان کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ اس کا شریک خوشحال ہے۔ پس مفلس نے غلام کو کمائی کرنے سے بری کر دیا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اس غلام کی ولاء تمام صورتوں میں موقوف رہے گی۔ کیونکہ ہر ایک اس کے آزاد کرنے کو دوسرے پر ڈالتا ہے اور دوسرا اس سے انکار کرتا ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اس کی ولاء موقوف رہے گی۔ یہاں تک کہ دونوں شریک کسی ایک کے آزاد کرنے پر اتفاق کریں۔ پس جس پر اتفاق ہو جائے گا غلام کی ولاء اسی کی ہوگی۔
Book of Emancipation. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary, volume 5. Similarly, if one of the two is wealthy and the other is poor, because each of them thinks that his partner freed his share, then according to his presumption, in the view of Imam Abu Hanifa, this slave becomes a mukatab, and it becomes forbidden to keep him as a slave. So he is believed as far as his own right is concerned. Therefore he will be prevented from enslaving the entire slave, and he will make the slave earn, because we are certain that he has the right to make him earn, whether he is truthful or false. Because this slave is his mukatab or his mamluk. Therefore each of the two can make him earn, and this ruling does not differ due to prosperity or poverty. Because the right of the master in both cases is one of two things: because the prosperity of the manumitter, according to Imam Sahib, does not prevent earning, and taking compensation from the partner is impossible due to his denial, so the second option is determined, i.e., making the slave earn. And the wala of this slave belongs to both partners, because each of them says that the other's share was freed by his manumission and his wala is mine. And the Sahibayn said: If both partners are wealthy, earning is not obligatory upon the slave, because each partner discharges the slave from earning, due to the fact that he claims compensation from his partner. Because according to the Sahibayn, the wealth of the manumitter prevents the slave from earning. But because the claim was not proven due to the other's denial, the slave's discharge from earning is established because his acknowledgment is binding upon himself. And if both partners are poor, the slave will earn for both, because each partner is a claimant of earnings from the slave, whether he is truthful or false, as we have stated, because the manumitter is insolvent. And if one of them is wealthy and the other is poor, the slave will earn for the wealthy one, because he does not claim compensation from his partner due to his poverty, but rather claims earnings from the slave, so the slave will not be discharged from him. And he will not earn for the poor partner, because he claims compensation from his partner due to his wealth. So the poor partner discharges the slave from earning. And according to the Sahibayn, in all these cases the wala of this slave remains suspended, because each of them attributes his manumission to the other, and the other disowns it. So his wala remains suspended until both partners agree on the manumission of one of them. Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that if one of the two partners acknowledges to the other that he freed the slave, then in that case it is obligatory upon the slave to earn the price of each share and pay it, whether both are prosperous or poor. This is the madhhab of Imam Sahib. And the same ruling applies when one of them is wealthy and the other is insolvent. The proof is that each of the two partners thinks that the other partner freed his share. Therefore, according to his thought, this slave became a mukatab, as is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. Now it becomes forbidden to enslave him. So the partner's presumption, even if not correct with respect to the other partner, is accepted with respect to himself. For this reason, he will be prevented from enslaving this slave, but rather he will make him earn, because we are certain that he has the right to make him earn, whether he is truthful in his statement or false. Because if this person is truthful, the slave becomes a mukatab, and if false, the slave is mamluk, and the earnings of the mamluk belong to his master. Therefore, whether the slave is mukatab or mamluk, each of the two partners can make him earn, and this ruling does not differ due to prosperity or poverty. Because the right of the one who claimed that his partner freed his share is, in both cases, one of two things: in the case of prosperity, taking compensation or making him earn. Because the prosperity of the manumitter, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, does not prevent making the slave earn, and here taking compensation is impossible because this partner denies the manumission, rather he claims that his partner freed the slave. So when taking compensation becomes impossible, the other option remains, which is to make the slave earn. And the wala of this slave belongs to both partners, because each partner says that the other's share was freed by his manumission, whose wala is his, and my share was freed by earning. And in this issue, the Sahibayn said: If both partners are wealthy, earning is not obligatory upon the slave, because each partner discharges the slave from earning, due to the fact that he claims compensation from his partner. Because according to the Sahibayn, when the manumitter is wealthy, earning (saiyah) is not obligatory upon the slave. But his claim against his partner was not established because he denies, and the slave's discharge from earning is established because his acknowledgment is binding upon himself. And if both partners are insolvent, the slave will earn for both, because each partner is a claimant of earnings from the slave, whether truthful or false, because the obligation of earning upon the slave is certain since the manumitter is insolvent. And if one of them is prosperous and the other insolvent, the slave will earn for the prosperous one, because he does not claim compensation from his partner due to his poverty, but rather claims earnings from the slave, so the slave will not be discharged from him. And he will not earn for the insolvent partner, because he claims compensation from his partner due to his partner's prosperity. So the insolvent partner has discharged the slave from earning. And according to the Sahibayn, in all cases the wala of this slave remains suspended, because each attributes his manumission to the other, and the other denies it. Therefore his wala remains suspended until both partners agree on the manumission of one of them. Then whoever they agree upon, the slave's wala will be his.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the obligation of a slave to earn for his two co-owners when one of them claims that the other has freed his share. The central issue: if one partner says to the other, 'You freed your share of our slave,' or acknowledges that the other freed his share, what are the consequences for the slave's earnings (saiyah) and his wala (clientage)? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that regardless of the wealth or poverty of the partners, the slave must earn for both partners the value of each share. This is because each partner's claim, even if possibly false, is taken as true with respect to his own rights. Thus the slave is considered a mukatab (contractually freeing himself) in relation to that partner, and it becomes forbidden for that partner to treat him as a full slave. Since the other partner denies freeing his share, compensation (daman) cannot be claimed from him, so the only way for the partner to recover his share is through the slave's earnings. The wala of the slave belongs to both partners: each claims that the other's share was freed by the other's manumission, and his own share is freed through the slave's earnings. The Sahibayn differentiate based on the financial status of the partners. If both are wealthy, each partner claims compensation from the other (since they are able to pay), so they discharge the slave from earning. The slave's discharge is established because each partner's acknowledgment (that the other freed his share) is binding on himself, but the claim against the other fails due to denial. If both are poor, each partner claims earnings from the slave, so the slave must earn for both because the manumitter is insolvent and cannot compensate. If one is wealthy and the other poor, the wealthy partner claims earnings from the slave (not compensation from the poor partner), so the slave earns for him; the poor partner claims compensation from the wealthy partner (since he is able to pay), so he discharges the slave from earning for him. In all cases according to the Sahibayn, the wala of the slave is suspended until the partners agree on who actually manumitted the slave. This passage also includes a detailed restatement of the scenario and reasoning. The commentary follows the order of the original text, first explaining the situation, then presenting Abu Hanifa's position with its proof, then the Sahibayn's ruling with its conditions.

Key Terms
سعایة (saiyah): The earnings of a slave that he must pay to his master, often to purchase his freedom or compensate for a share of his value.
ولاء (wala): The clientage relationship between a freed slave and his manumitter, entailing inheritance rights and responsibility.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), what is the ruling on the slave's earnings if both partners are wealthy?
A) The slave must earn for both partners.
B) The slave must earn for neither partner because each claims compensation from the other.
C) The slave must earn only for the wealthy partner.
D) The slave must earn for the partner who manumitted.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the slave's obligation to earn when a partner claims the other freed his share depends on whether you follow Abu Hanifa (always earn for both) or the Sahibayn (depends on wealth), with wala being immediate or suspended accordingly.

Conditional Emancipation by Two Partners with Opposite Conditions
Ruling on contradictory conditional emancipation by co-owners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two co-owners of a slave impose opposite conditional emancipation statements and the occurrence of the condition is unknown, and explain the basis for partial emancipation and sa'yah.

Core Idea
When two owners make opposite conditional emancipation statements, uncertainty leads to half emancipation and the slave earns for the remaining half.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh regarding conditional emancipation (itaq) by co-owners. When two partners each impose a condition on the emancipation of their jointly owned slave, and the conditions are contradictory, the ruling depends on whether the condition occurred. If it becomes impossible to know, a precise legal remedy is applied. Understanding this case reinforces principles of uncertainty and partial ownership in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and conditions
Two partners co-own a slave. Partner 1 says: 'If so-and-so does not enter this house tomorrow, the slave is free.' Partner 2 says: 'If so-and-so enters, the slave is free.' The conditions are opposite.
2
Recognize the uncertainty
The next day passes, and it becomes unknown whether the person entered the house or not. Thus, it is impossible to determine which condition was fulfilled.
3
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, half of the slave is freed (because one condition must have occurred), and the remaining half is still slave. For that half, the slave must work (sa'yah) to earn money for both partners.
The sa'yah compensates the owners for the value of the unfreed half.
4
Note the incomplete opinion of Muhammad
The source text ends with 'And Muhammad said...' indicating that Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani likely held a different view, but his opinion is not provided here.
This highlights an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال احد الشريكين ان لم يدخل فلان هذه الدار غدا فهو حر وقال الأخر ان دخل فهو حر فمضى الغدو لا يدرى دخل ام لا عتق النصف وسعى لهما في النصف وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وقال محمد
And if one of the two partners says, 'If so-and-so does not enter this house tomorrow, he is free,' and the other says, 'If he enters, he is free,' then the next day passes and it is not known whether he entered or not, half of the slave is freed and he works for both for the [other] half. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said...
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This is a matn (text) from the Hidayah stating a ruling on conditional emancipation by two co-owners of a slave. They impose opposite conditions: one says the slave is free if a specified person does NOT enter a house tomorrow; the other says the slave is free if that person DOES enter. The next day passes and it becomes unknown whether the condition occurred. Since it is impossible to determine which condition was fulfilled, both conditions are considered partially effective: half the slave is freed (one condition succeeded, but which half? The text says half), and for the remaining half that is still slave, the slave must earn (saiyah) for both partners. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The text ends mid-sentence with 'And Muhammad said...' indicating that Muhammad al-Shaybani may have a different opinion, but it is not provided here. The ruling is concise and meant for memorization.

Key Terms
عتق (itaq): Emancipation of a slave, making him free.
سعى (sa'yah): The act of a slave working to earn money for the master, often to pay for his own emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The entire slave is freed.
B) Half the slave is freed and the slave earns for the other half.
C) The slave is not freed at all.
D) The slave is freed only if both partners agree.

Key Takeaway
When two co-owners make opposite conditional emancipation statements and the condition is unknown, half the slave is freed and the other half requires sa'yah.

Contradictory Oaths on Slave Freedom: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on joint slave with contradictory conditional oaths and sa'y

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on sa'y when two co-owners make contradictory conditional oaths and identify the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Certainty of half-freedom requires half-sa'y; ignorance of which master is freed is resolved by apportionment.

When two co-owners make conditional oaths that conflict, and the condition's occurrence is unknown, we face a puzzle: the slave is partially free, but which part? This passage presents the Hanafi solution and an intra-madhhab disagreement between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the facts: two co-owners of a slave, one says 'if X does not enter, the slave is free', the other says 'if X enters, the slave is free'. The condition's occurrence is unknown.
This is a classic case of contradictory conditional oaths.
2
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
The two shaykhs rule that half the slave is freed automatically, and the slave must perform sa'y (earning work) for both masters for the remaining half-value.
The reasoning: one oath is certainly broken, so half freedom is certain; the other half remains, and sa'y compensates for it.
3
Identify the opposing view of Muhammad
Imam Muhammad rules that the slave must perform sa'y for the entire value, because the master against whom sa'y is dropped is unknown, and judgment cannot be passed on an unknown.
He analogizes to a debt claim against an unknown person.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Muhammad
The two shaykhs respond that certainty of half sa'y being dropped is established, so imposing full sa'y is contradictory; ignorance is removed by apportionment (shuyu' wa tawzi'), as in the case of freeing one of two unspecified slaves.
Apportionment resolves the unknown without injustice.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical difference
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: half free, half sa'y. The practical difference: under Muhammad, the slave would work to pay the full value; under the two shaykhs, only half.
This affects the slave's workload and the masters' compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال احد الشريكين ان لم يدخل فلان هذه الدار غدا فهو حر وقال الأخر ان دخل فهو حر فمضى الغد لا يدرى دخل ام لا عتق النصف وسعى لهما في النصف وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وقال محمد يسعى في جميع قيمته لان المقضى عليه بسقوط السعاية مجهول ولا يمكن القضاء على المجهول فصار كما اذا قال لغيره لك على احدنا الف درهم فانه لا يقضى بشئ للجهالة كذا هذا ولهما انا تيقنا بسقوط نصف السعاية لان احد هما حانث بيقين ومع التيقن بسقوط النصف كيف يقضى بوجوب الكل و الجهالة ترتفع بالشيوع والتوزيع كما اذا اعتق احد عبديه لا بعينه أو بعينه ونسيه ومات قبل التذكر او البيان ويتاتى التفريع فيه على ان اليسار هل يمنع السعاية اولا يمنعها على الاختلاف الذي سبق
And if one of the two partners said: 'If so-and-so does not enter this house tomorrow, then he (the slave) is free,' and the other said: 'If he enters, then he is free,' then the morrow passes and it is not known whether he entered or not: half the slave is freed, and he (the slave) performs sa'y (earning work) for both of them for the half. And this according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: He performs sa'y for the entire value, because the one decreed to have the sa'y dropped is unknown, and judgment cannot be passed upon the unknown. So it is like when someone says to another: 'One of us owes you one thousand dirhams' — then nothing is decreed due to ignorance. Likewise this. And their (the two's) proof: we are certain that half the sa'y is dropped because one of the two is certainly oath-breaker; with certainty of half being dropped, how can one decree the obligation of the whole? Ignorance is removed by distribution and apportionment, as when one frees one of his two slaves unspecified, or specifies but forgets, and dies before recalling or clarifying. And the ramification is built upon whether wealth prevents sa'y or not, according to the difference that preceded.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where two co-owners of a slave make contradictory conditional oaths regarding the slave's freedom—one says 'if X does not enter the house, the slave is free' and the other says 'if X enters, the slave is free.' After the day passes, it is unknown whether the condition occurred. The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), half of the slave is freed automatically because one of the oaths is certainly broken, but we cannot determine which. Therefore, the slave becomes half-free and must perform sa'y (work to compensate) for both masters for the remaining half-value. Imam Muhammad argues that the slave must perform sa'y for the entire value because the person against whom sa'y is dropped is unknown (i.e., which master's share is freed), and judgment cannot be made on an unknown. He analogizes to a debt of one thousand dirhams claimed against one of two unknown persons—no judgment is given due to ignorance. The two shaykhs respond that certainty of half sa'y being dropped is established, so imposing full sa'y is contradictory; ignorance is resolved by apportionment, like the case of freeing one of two unspecified slaves and dying before specification. The passage also notes that the effect of wealth (yasar) on sa'y is to be considered based on earlier differences—if the freeing master is wealthy, sa'y is not required from the slave, but here the issue is complicated by uncertainty about who is the freeing master.

Key Terms
سعاية (sa'y): Earning work performed by a slave to pay off his value or compensation to the master(s) as part of the process of freedom.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks an oath (yamin). Here, one of the two co-owners certainly broke his oath regarding the condition for freedom.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about a specific fact that affects the ruling—here, which co-owner's oath was broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when two co-owners make contradictory conditional oaths regarding the freedom of their jointly owned slave and the condition's occurrence is unknown, what is the ruling?
A) The slave remains fully enslaved until the condition is clarified.
B) Half the slave is freed, and he must perform sa'y for both masters for the remaining half.
C) The slave is completely free because one of the oaths is certainly broken.
D) The slave must perform sa'y for the full value to compensate both masters.

Key Takeaway
When contradictory conditional oaths leave one break certain but the specific breaker unknown, Hanafi jurisprudence frees half the slave and imposes half-sa'y, resolving ignorance by apportionment.

Two Oaths on Two Slaves: Judgment Withheld Due to Extreme Ignorance
Ruling on contradictory oaths on separate slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two men swear contradictory conditional oaths on separate slaves and explain why extreme ignorance (jahalah fahishah) prevents the judge from emancipating any slave.

Core Idea
When both master and slave are indeterminate from contradictory oaths, extreme ignorance blocks the judge's ruling.

This passage from the Book of Emancipation (Kitab al-'Itaq) addresses a scenario where two men each own separate slaves and swear opposite oaths about Zaid's entry into a house. After the day passes, the condition's occurrence is unknown. The Hanafi school rules that no slave is freed. Let us explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the concept of extreme ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and oaths
Two men each own a separate slave. One swears, 'If Zaid enters the house tomorrow, my slave is free.' The other swears, 'If Zaid does not enter, my slave is free.'
The oaths are conditional and contradictory.
2
Recognize the impossibility of ascertainment
After the day passes, no one knows whether Zaid entered or not. The condition's occurrence is unknown.
The fact is indeterminable.
3
Understand extreme ignorance (jahalah fahishah)
Both the master against whom the ruling would be applied (al-maqḍā ʿalayhi) and the slave who would be freed (al-maqḍā lahu) are unknown. This constitutes jahalah fahishah, which prevents judicial ruling.
Juridicial ignorance: the parties of the ruling are indeterminate.
4
Conclude why judgment is withheld
Because the ignorance is extreme, the judge cannot issue a ruling of emancipation. Both oaths are effectively inoperative.
The ruling is agreed upon (muttafaq ʿalayh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلفا على عبدين كل واحد منهما لا حد همالم يعتق واحد منهما لان المقضى عليه بالعتق مجهول و كذالک المقضى له فتفاحشت الجهالة فامتنع القضاء وفى العبدالواحد المقضى به معلوم فغلب المعلوم المجهول
ترجمه اگر دو شخصوں نے دو غلاموں پر قسم کھائی ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک نے ایک کے واسطے تو ان دونوں میں سے کوئی آزاد نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ جس پر آزاد ہونے کا حکم لگایا گیا وہ مجہول ہے اور ایسے ہی مقتضی لۂ ( غلام بھی مجہول ہے ) تو جہالت بہت بڑھ گئی ۔ پس قضاء ممتنع ہو گئی اور ایک غلام میں مقضیٰ یہ معلوم ہے۔ پس معلوم مجہول پر غالب آیا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ دو شخصوں میں سے ہر ایک کیلئے علیحدہ علیحدہ غلام ہے۔ پس ان میں سے ایک نے کہا ان دخل زید هذه الدار غداً فعبدي حر اور دوسرے نے کہا ان لم يدخل زيد هذه الدار غداً فعبدی حر پھر کل کا دن گذر گیا اور یہ دریافت نہ ہوسکتا کہ زید گھر میں داخل ہو ایا داخل نہیں ہوا تو اس صورت میں کوئی غلام آزاد نہ ہوگا اور یہ حکم متفق علیہ ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جس مولیٰ پر یہ حکم دیا جائے کہ اس کا غلام آزاد ہوا ہے۔ وہ مجہول ہے اور اسی طرح جو غلام آزاد ہوا ہے وہ بھی مجہول ہے۔ تو یہاں جہالت فاحشہ پائی گئی۔ پس اس جہالت فاحشہ کی وجہ سے قاضی کچھ کم نہیں دے سکتا اور اگر ایک غلام میں یہ کلام ہوتا تو وہ غلام معلوم تھا۔ صرف یہ مجہول تھا کہ کس شریک پر یہ حکم ہو۔ لہذا اس صورت میں معلوم کو مجہول پر غالب کر کے قاضی حکم دے دیتا ہے۔
And if two people take an oath regarding two slaves, each one of them [swearing] for the other, none of them will be freed, because the one against whom the ruling of emancipation is made is unknown, and likewise the one in whose favor it is made is unknown, so the ignorance becomes excessive, and therefore judgment is withheld. In the case of a single slave, the one about whom the ruling is made is known, so the known prevails over the unknown.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the book of emancipation (Kitab al-'Itaq). The scenario: two men each own a separate slave. One swears, 'If Zaid enters this house tomorrow, my slave is free.' The other swears, 'If Zaid does not enter this house tomorrow, my slave is free.' After the day passes, it is impossible to know whether Zaid entered or not. The ruling, agreed upon by all, is that neither slave is emancipated. The reason: both the person against whom the ruling is applied (the master whose slave is to be freed) and the beneficiary (the slave who would be freed) are unknown due to the contradictory oaths. This extreme ignorance (jahalah fahishah) prevents the judge from issuing a ruling. In contrast, if there were only one slave and the oath was about that slave's owner, the slave is known, so the judge can rule by giving precedence to the known over the unknown, as in the earlier ruling (page 318) where a single slave was half-freed.

Key Terms
مقضى عليه (maqḍā ʿalayhi): The person against whom the ruling of emancipation is made; the master whose slave is to be freed.
مقضى له (maqḍā lahu): The person in whose favor the ruling is made; the slave who would be freed.
جهالة فاحشة (jahālah fāḥishah): Extreme ignorance that prevents a valid judicial ruling because the party or subject is indeterminable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the judge not emancipate any slave in the case of two men swearing contradictory oaths on two different slaves?
A) Because the oaths are invalid from the start.
B) Because both the debtor and beneficiary of the ruling are unknown, causing extreme ignorance.
C) Because the slaves are not present in court.
D) Because the judge cannot decide between the two masters.

Key Takeaway
When both the party and the subject of a judicial ruling are unknown due to contradictory conditions, extreme ignorance prevents the judge from issuing a ruling.

Contrast with the Single-Slave Case: Known Prevails Over Unknown
Contrasting ruling and legal maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two-slave case from the single-slave case and explain the legal principle that the known (al-ma'lum) prevails over the unknown (al-majhul).

Core Idea
In the single-slave case, since the slave is known, the judge can rule by giving precedence to the known over the unknown.

This passage contrasts the previous ruling with a simpler scenario: when only one slave is involved and the oath is about that slave's owner. Here, the slave is known, so the judge can overcome the remaining ignorance. This introduces an important legal maxim: the known prevails over the unknown.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two-slave case
Previously, with two slaves and two contradictory oaths, both the master and slave were unknown, leading to jahalah fahishah and no emancipation.
That case established the principle that extreme ignorance blocks judgment.
2
Compare with the single-slave scenario
Now consider: only one slave exists, and the oaths are about that slave's owner. The slave itself is a known entity.
The slave is the maqḍā lahu (beneficiary).
3
Identify the known element
The slave is known. The only unknown is which master's oath is true. This is jahalah yasirah (minor ignorance) because the subject is determined.
The unknown is the maqḍā ʿalayhi (the master), not the slave.
4
Apply the principle that the known prevails
Since the slave is known, the judge rules by freeing the slave (or half-freeing) rather than refusing altogether. The known overcomes the unknown.
This is the maxim 'al-ma'lum yaghlib al-majhul' (the known prevails over the unknown).

Checkpoint
In the single-slave case, why can the judge rule even though the condition is unknown?
A) Because the judge can guess which master's oath is true.
B) Because the slave is known and the maxim 'the known prevails over the unknown' applies.
C) Because the oaths are not contradictory.
D) Because the day has passed and the condition is no longer relevant.

Key Takeaway
When ignorance is partial and the subject is known, the judge can rule by giving precedence to the known over the unknown.

The Father's Joint Purchase of His Son: Liability Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for joint purchase of a relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a father must compensate his partner when they jointly purchase the father's son, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Joint purchase of a relative creates implicit consent; Abu Hanifa frees the father from liability, the Sahibayn impose it.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute in the chapter of emancipation. The scenario: a father and another person jointly purchase a slave who is the son of the father. The father's share is automatically freed because purchasing a close relative is legally emancipation. The dispute is whether the father must compensate his co-purchaser for the loss of the partner's share. We will examine the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn and understand the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Two men jointly purchase a slave who is the son of one of them. The father's share is automatically freed because purchasing a close relative is considered emancipation (itaq) in Hanafi fiqh.
This ruling is agreed upon; the dispute is about the consequences.
2
State the point of dispute
The dispute is whether the father is liable to compensate his partner for the value of the partner's share that is now lost due to the automatic emancipation.
3
Present Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the father owes no compensation. The partner implicitly consented by entering into the purchase, which is the cause of emancipation. This is analogous to explicitly giving permission to free the share.
The partner's act of joining the purchase implies consent to the inevitable consequence.
4
Present the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the father must compensate. If the father is wealthy, he pays half the slave's value; if insolvent, the slave earns half the value for the partner. They analogize to the case of two strangers co-owning a slave where one frees his share.
They argue that the father's purchase only emancipates his own share; freeing the whole is an act of destroying the partner's property without consent.
5
Identify the tarjih (relied-upon view)
The text states that Abu Hanifa's view is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) from him. The ruling revolves around the cause, and the partner's awareness does not affect the outcome.
Thus, the mufta bihi is that the father is not liable, and the partner has the option to free his share or demand istis'ā' from the slave.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ، اگر باپ اور ایک دوسرے شخص نے مل کر اپنا بیٹا خریدا تو باپ کا حصہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنے
And if two men purchase the son of one of them, the father's share is freed because he has become the owner of a share of his relative, and purchasing him is emancipation as has passed, and there is no guarantee upon him whether the other knows that he is the son of his partner or does not know. Likewise if they inherit him. The partner has the option: if he wishes he may free his share, and if he wishes he may make the slave earn (istis'ā'). This is according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: in the case of purchase, the father guarantees half of his value if he is wealthy; if he is insolvent, the son earns half of his value for the partner of his father. And the same dispute applies when they own him by gift, or windfall, or bequest. And likewise when two men purchase him and one of them had sworn upon his emancipation if he purchased his half. They argued: he nullified the share of his partner by emancipation, because purchase of a relative is emancipation, and it becomes as if the slave were between two strangers and one of them freed his share. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: the partner consented to the corruption of his share, so he cannot claim guarantee, as if he had explicitly permitted him to free his share. The evidence of this is that he partnered with him in what is the cause of emancipation, namely purchase, because purchase of a relative is emancipation, so that he is relieved of the obligation of expiation according to us. And this is a guarantee for corruption in the apparent opinion of them (the Sahibayn), so that it varies with wealth and insolvency; thus it falls by consent. And the ruling does not differ between knowledge and ignorance, and this is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) from him, because the ruling revolves around the cause, just as if someone said to another 'eat this food' while it was owned by the commander and the commander does not know he owns it.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute in the chapter of emancipation. The scenario: a father and another person jointly purchase a slave who is the son of the father. Upon purchase, the father's share is automatically emancipated because purchasing a close relative (such as one's child) is legally considered an act of emancipation (shira' al-qarib 'itaq). This ruling is agreed upon; the dispute is about whether the father must compensate his co-purchaser for the value of the partner's share that is lost due to the emancipation. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the father is not liable for any compensation. The partner has the option to either free his own share or make the slave work to earn the value (istis'ā'). Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the partner willingly entered into the purchase knowing that purchasing a relative causes emancipation; by partnering in that purchase, he implicitly consented to the loss of his share. This is analogous to explicitly giving permission to free the share. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that the father must compensate the partner. If the father is wealthy, he pays half the slave's value; if insolvent, the slave must earn that amount. Their reasoning: the father's purchase of his son is a legal cause for emancipation, but it only directly applies to the father's own share; by freeing the whole slave, he destroys the partner's property without consent. This is analogous to the case where two strangers co-own a slave and one frees his share — the freed slave would have to earn for the other partner's share. The text then notes that Abu Hanifa's position is the relied-upone (zahir al-riwayah) in this context, because the ruling is based on the cause (purchase) and the partner's consent is inferred. The passage ends with an analogy: if someone tells another to eat food that is actually owned by the speaker, but the speaker is unaware of his ownership, the eater is not liable. Similarly, the father's purchase is a cause of emancipation, and the partner's awareness or ignorance does not affect the ruling.

Key Terms
شراء القريب (shirā' al-qarīb): Purchase of a close relative (e.g., son, father) which is legally considered an emancipation.
استسعاء (istis'ā'): A ruling that a partially freed slave must work to earn the value of the remaining unfreed portion to secure full freedom.
ضمان إفساد (ḍamān ifsād): Compensation for causing corruption or loss to someone's property. Here, the father is liable for the partner's share loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the father not required to compensate his partner when their jointly purchased slave turns out to be the father's son?
A) Because the father did not intend to free the partner's share.
B) Because the partner implicitly consented to the loss by entering into the purchase, which is the cause of emancipation.
C) Because the partner is also a relative of the slave.
D) Because the father is insolvent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a father jointly purchases his son, his share is automatically freed, and the relied-upon view is that he owes no compensation because the partner consented by joining the purchase.

The Father’s Share in a Son-Slave: Liability or Option?
Ruling on the father's share freeing and the stranger's options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the father's liability when his share of a jointly-owned son-slave becomes free, and apply the rule of consent to similar cases.

Core Idea
Consent nullifies liability: Abu Hanifa holds the stranger agreed to the risk; the Sahibayn hold emancipation indivisible.

This lesson explores a scenario where a father and a stranger jointly own a slave who is the father's son. When the father's share becomes free by operation of law (purchasing a mahram relative), a dispute arises: should the father compensate the stranger for his lost share? Imam Abu Hanifa says no, but the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) say yes under certain conditions. We will analyze both positions and their proofs to understand the underlying principles of consent and indivisibility of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the exact scenario: father and stranger jointly own a slave who is the father's son. The father's share becomes free because purchasing a mahram relative automatically emancipates. The issue is what happens to the stranger's share.
The scenario applies both to purchase and inheritance, but the dispute primarily concerns purchase.
2
Outline Abu Hanifa's Position
According to Abu Hanifa, the father is not liable. The stranger has an option: either free his own share or make the slave work (istis'a') to pay the stranger the value. The proof: the stranger consented to the destruction of his share by partnering with the father in a transaction that necessarily frees the slave.
Consent is implied from the nature of the partnership; knowledge of the relationship is irrelevant.
3
Outline the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn distinguish between inheritance (they agree with Abu Hanifa) and purchase. In purchase, if the father is wealthy, he must guarantee half the slave's price to the stranger. If poor, the slave works. Their proof: emancipation is indivisible (la yatajuzza), so freeing one's share nullifies the other's share, triggering compensation.
This is analogous to two strangers co-owning a slave when one frees his share.
4
Compare and Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference (thamarah) is whether the father must pay if wealthy. According to Abu Hanifa, never; according to Sahibayn, yes if father is wealthy. Also, the stranger's right to demand istis'a' differs: Abu Hanifa gives the stranger the option; Sahibayn only allow if father is poor.
The dispute hinges on the concept of consent vs. indivisibility of emancipation.

Source Text

قرابتدار کے جز کا مالک ہوا اور قرابتی کو خریدنا آزاد کرنا ہوتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ فصل من ملک ذا رحم محرم میں گذر چکا ہے اور باپ پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا ۔ خواہ شریک جانتا ہو کہ یہ اس کا بیٹا ہے یا نہ جانتا ہو اور یہی حکم اس وقت ہے جبکہ ان دونوں نا اموا نے یہ غلام میراث میں پایا ہو۔ یعنی باپ کا حصہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اس کی صورت یہ ہوگی کہ ایک عورت نے اپنے شوہر کے بہ کو خریدا۔ پھر یہ عورت مرگئی اور اس نے اپنا شوہر اور بھائی چھوڑا۔ تو اس غلام کا نصف شوہر کیلئے ہوگا اور وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ پھر باپ کے علاوہ دوسرے شریک کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ چاہے اپنا حصہ آزاد کر دے اور چاہے غلام سے کمائی کرا کے اپنی قیمت لے لے۔ یہ حضرت امام اعظم کا قول ہے۔
اور صاحبین نے کہا میراث کی صورت میں تو وہی حکم ہے۔ لیکن خریدنے کی صورت میں اگر باپ خوشحال ہو تو بیٹے کی آدھی قیمت کا شریک کیلئے ضامن ہو گا اور گر باپ مفلس ہو تو بیٹا آدمی قیمت اس کو کمائی کر کے ادا کرے اور یہی اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے جبکہ باپ اور دوسرا شخص اس غلام کا مالک بطور ہبہ ہوا ہے۔ بایں طور کہ کسی شخص نے ان دونوں کے واسطے اس غلام کی وصیت کی ہو اور یہی اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے کہ دو مردوں نے اس کو خریدا۔ حالانکہ ایک نے یہ قسم کھائی تھی۔ کہ اگر میں اس غلام کا آدھا خر یدوں تو وہ آزاد ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ باپ نے اپنا حصہ آزاد کر کے اپنے ساتھی کا حصہ باطل کر دیا۔ کیونکہ قریبی عزیز کا خرید نا آزاد کرنا ہوتا ہے اور یہ ایسی صورت ہوگئی جیسے ایک غلام دو اجنبی آدمیوں کے درمیان مشترک ہو۔ پھر ان میں سے ایک اپنا حصہ آزاد کر دے۔ پس چونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک اعتاق متجری نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اگر آزاد کرنے والا خوشحال ہے تو اپنے ساتھی کے حصہ کا ضامن ہوگا اور اگر مفلس ہے تو غلام کمائی کر کے نصف قیمت ادا کرے گا۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شریک آخر اپنا حصہ خراب ہونے پر راضی ہو گیا تھا اور رضامندی کے ساتھ ظلم جمع نہیں ہوتا ۔ لہذا وہ باپ سے تاوان نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ جیسے ایک شریک دوسرے شریک کو اس کا حصہ آزاد کرنے کی اجازت صریح دیدے اور شریک آخر کی اپنے حصہ کے خراب ہونے پر رضا مندی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شریک نے غلام کے باپ کے ساتھ ایسی چیز میں شرکت کی ہے جو آزاد ہو جانے کی علت ہے۔ یعنی خریداری میں کیونکہ قریبی عزیز کو خریدنا ، آزاد کرنا ہوتا ہے۔
اس عبارت میں تسامح ہے۔ وہ یہ کہ مصنف ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ شراء قریب علت عشق ہے۔ حالانکہ شراء قریب ملک کی علت ہے اور ملک علت عشق ہے۔ پس شراء قریب عشق کی علت نہیں بلکہ علت العلت ہے۔ مگر اس تسامح کا جواب یہ ہوگا کہ حکم جس طرح علت کی طرف منسوب کیا جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح علت العلت کی طرف بھی منسوب کیا جاتا ہے۔ لہذا ثابت ہوا کہ قریبی عزیز کو خریدنے سے وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا ۔ چنانچہ اگر اس پر کفارہ کا غلام ہو تو قریبی عزیز کو خریدنے سے ہمارے نزدیک کفارہ ادا ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین کا ظاہری قول یہ کہ یہ ضمان افساد ہے نہ کہ ضمان تملک حتی کہ بیمار اور اعسار کی وجہ سے مختلف ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس جب شریک اس خراب ہونے پر راضی ہو تو ضمان ساتھ ہو گیا ۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ شریک کو معلوم ہو کہ یہ اس کا بیٹا ہے یا معلوم نہ ہو دونوں صورتوں میں یہی حکم ہے اور یہ حکم امام ابو حنیفہ سے ظاہر وابستہ ہے ۔ کیونکہ حکم کا مدار علت پر ہے۔ جیسے کسی نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ یہ کھانا کھالے۔ حالانکہ یہ کھانا آمر کی ملک ہے اور آمر کو اس کا تم بھی نہیں تھا کہ یہ کھانا میرا ملک ہے۔ پس ما موز نے وہ کھانا کھا لیا تو مامور آمر کیلئے کسی چیز کا ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ اگر چہ آمر اس پر راضی نہیں ہے اور راضی ن ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے اس کو علم نہیں ہے۔
He who becomes owner of a portion of a close relative, and purchasing a close relative effects emancipation. As has passed in the chapter on owning a mahram relative. And no guarantee is due upon the father, whether the partner knows that this is his son or not. And this is the ruling when they both inherited this slave, i.e., the father's share becomes free. An example: a woman bought her husband's father (?) then she died, leaving her husband and brother. So half of the slave goes to the husband, and he becomes free. Then the other partner besides the father has the option: either he frees his share or he makes the slave work (s{u}aya) and takes his price. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. And the Sahibayn said: in the case of inheritance, the same ruling; but in the case of purchase, if the father is wealthy, he is responsible (damin) for half the price to the partner, and if the father is poor, the slave works and pays the partner. And this same difference applies when the father and the other person become owners of the slave by gift, such that someone bequeathed the slave to both. And this same difference applies when two men bought him, while one had sworn: 'If I buy half of this slave, he is free.' The proof of the Sahibayn: The father freed his share, thus nullifying his partner's share, because purchasing a close relative effects emancipation, and this becomes like a slave jointly owned by two strangers, then one frees his share. Since, according to the Sahibayn, emancipation is not partitionable, therefore if the manumitter is wealthy, he is responsible for his partner's share, and if he is poor, the slave works and pays half the price. And the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa: The partner agreed to the destruction of his share, and oppression cannot coexist with consent, so he cannot take compensation from the father. Just as if one partner gives clear permission to the other to free his share. And the evidence of the partner's consent to the destruction of his share is that he entered into partnership with the slave's father in that which is the cause of emancipation, i.e., in the purchase, because purchasing a close relative effects emancipation. There is a laxity in this expression: the author of Hidayah said that purchase of a close relative is the cause of emancipation, whereas purchase is the cause of ownership and ownership is the cause of emancipation. So purchase of a close relative is not the cause of emancipation but rather the cause of the cause. But the answer to this laxity is that just as a ruling is attributed to the cause, similarly it is attributed to the cause of the cause. Hence it is established that by purchasing a close relative he becomes free. Thus, if a person owes a slave for atonement (kaffara), by purchasing a close relative the atonement is fulfilled according to us. And the apparent saying of the Sahibayn is that this guarantee is a guarantee of corruption (daman ifsad), not a guarantee of ownership (daman tamalluk), such that it varies due to illness and poverty. So when the partner agrees to this corruption, the guarantee goes along. The author of Hidayah said: regardless of whether the partner knows that this is his son or does not know, in both cases the ruling is the same. And this ruling is apparently connected to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the ruling hinges on the cause. For example, if someone says to another, 'Eat this food,' while the food is the property of the commander, and the commander was not aware that the food is his property? (The analogy is unclear). Then the commanded ate the food, so the commanded is not responsible for anything to the commander, even if the commander is not pleased with it, and the proof of his displeasure is that he did not know.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the case where a father and a stranger jointly own a slave who is the father's son. The key issue: when the father's share becomes free due to his ownership of his son (purchasing a close relative effects emancipation), what happens to the stranger's share? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the stranger has the option to either free his own share or force the slave to work (istis'a') to pay the stranger the value of his share. The father is not liable for any guarantee. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish between inheritance and purchase: in inheritance, they agree; in purchase, if the father is wealthy, he must guarantee half the price to the stranger; if poor, the slave works. The reason for the difference is that the Sahibayn view emancipation as indivisible; by freeing his share, the father effectively nullifies the stranger's share, so he must compensate. Abu Hanifa argues that the stranger consented to the destruction of his share by knowingly partnering with the father, who is a close relative; purchase of such a relative automatically frees, so the stranger is deemed to have accepted the risk. The text also notes a minor inaccuracy in the author of Hidayah's phrasing but defends it by explaining that rulings can be attributed to cause-of-the-cause. The illustration of eating food without permission is used to analogize consent and lack of knowledge.

Key Terms
شراء قریب (shira' qarib): The act of purchasing a close relative (mahram) which causes immediate emancipation of that relative.
اعتاق متجری (itq mutajazzi): The divisibility of emancipation; the Sahibayn hold that emancipation cannot be partitioned (la yatajuzza).
سعایہ (su'aya): The act of the slave working to earn money to pay the partner the value of his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when a father and a stranger jointly own a slave who is the father's son, and the father's share becomes free, what right does the stranger have?
A) The stranger can demand the father pay half the slave's price.
B) The stranger can either free his own share or make the slave work to earn the value.
C) The stranger must free his share and has no compensation.
D) The stranger can force the sale of the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the father is not liable for the stranger's share in a son-slave because the stranger's consent is inherent in the partnership, but the Sahibayn disagree based on the indivisibility of emancipation.
